The Asymmetry of Infinite Regress in Lack of Evidence
The author distinguishes between lack of evidence for existence and non-existence regarding infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why lack of evidence for existence does not lead to the same logical problem as lack of evidence for non-existence regarding infinite regress.

Core Idea
Affirming infinite things is impossible; negating infinite things is not.

This lesson resolves a key objection by showing an asymmetry between two cases. The author argues that while affirming an infinite series is impossible, merely negating one is not. This distinction saves the principle that lack of evidence for existence implies non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the two scenarios presented.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two cases
Look for the two conditional statements: one about lack of evidence for non-existence leading to affirmation of the infinite, and one about lack of evidence for existence leading to negation of the infinite.
The author uses 'يلزم هنا' and 'يلزم ثمة' to mark the two cases.
3
Understand the asymmetry
Notice that affirming the infinite is deemed impossible ('ممتنع'), whereas negating it is not impossible. This is the core distinction.
Think about why affirming an infinite series is problematic while negating it is not.
4
See how the objection is resolved
The passage concludes that the distinction becomes apparent and the difficulty is removed. Understand that the objection assumed symmetry, but the author shows asymmetry.
This reinforces the principle used in proving God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً يلزم هنا أي من كون عدم دليل النفي مستلزماً للوجود إثبات ما لا يتناهى وهو ممتنع ويلزم ثمة أي من كون عدم دليل الوجود مستلزماً للانتفاء نفيه أي نفي ما لا يتناهى ولا يمتنع هذا النفي فظهر الفرق واندفع الإشكال
And also, it follows here—i.e., from the fact that lack of evidence for non-existence entails existence—the affirmation of that which has no end, which is impossible. And it follows there—i.e., from the fact that lack of evidence for existence entails non-existence—the negation of that which has no end, and this negation is not impossible. Thus the distinction became apparent and the difficulty was removed.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a distinction that resolves the previous difficulty regarding infinite regress. The author argues that if lack of evidence for non-existence implied existence, then it would force us to affirm infinite things, which is impossible. On the other hand, if lack of evidence for existence implied non-existence, it would only force us to negate infinite things, and this negation is not impossible. Therefore, the two cases are not symmetrical, and the objection that they lead to contradiction is invalid. The distinction shows that the principle 'lack of evidence for existence implies non-existence' is acceptable because it only leads to negating the infinite, which is not problematic, while the opposite would lead to affirming the infinite, which is impossible.

Key Terms
ما لا يتناهى (infinite)
إثبات (affirmation)
نفي (negation)
امتناع (impossibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the two cases (lack of evidence for existence vs. non-existence) are symmetrical and both lead to impossibility.
Correction: This passage shows asymmetry: affirming infinite is impossible, but negating it is not.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about negating infinite things?
A) It is impossible.
B) It is not impossible.
C) It is necessary.
D) It is equivalent to affirming them.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that negating an infinite series is logically unproblematic, while affirming it is impossible, which resolves the objection against the principle of evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own reasoning about faith, do you sometimes assume that if something cannot be proven it must be false? How does this lesson's emphasis on the asymmetry between affirming and negating the infinite challenge you to re-evaluate that assumption?
?
Think of a time when you dismissed an argument because you believed it led to an infinite regress. Were you right to dismiss it? How can this lesson help you discern which infinite regresses are problematic and which are not?
?
This lesson distinguishes between impossible affirmation and permissible negation. In your spiritual life, are there truths about Allah that you have denied simply because they seemed to lead to an infinite chain? What would change if you considered that negating an infinite regress might still leave the original claim intact?

Critique of the 'No Preference' Argument for Divine Unity
Presents a common kalam argument: no number is preferable, so no number exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the kalam argument that denies multiplicity by citing absence of preference, and the author's critical stance towards it.

Core Idea
The argument that 'no number is preferable' aims to prove God's unity but is itself flawed.

This passage introduces the first of several premises that are widely used in kalam but are actually weak. The argument attempts to negate any number of gods beyond one by claiming that if there were multiple, no number would be more fitting than another – leading to the conclusion that no number exists at all. The author signals that this reasoning is not sound, and we will see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to the flow: the author announces a critique of famous premises.
The passage is short but dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the argument's logical steps
Break down the argument: (1) Theologians want to negate an infinite number to affirm the One. (2) They claim no number is more appropriate than another. (3) Therefore, number is negated entirely. (4) Hence, God is One.
This is a common proof for tawhid.
3
Contextualize the argument
Understand that this argument is used specifically to prove the oneness of God (unity). It is a typical kalam approach: negate any multiplicity by showing no basis for preference.
4
Reflect on the author's criticism
Notice that the author begins by saying 'let us speak about the weakness of premises'. So the argument is not accepted as sound. Think about why it might be weak – for example, the premise 'no number is preferable' might not be true or might lead to absurd conclusions.
We will see the full critique in subsequent passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنتكلم الآن في ضعف مقدمات مشهورة بين القوم أي المتكلمين ذوات فروع كثيرة من المسائل العظيمة الكلامية. الأولى أنهم إذا أرادوا نفي عدد غير متناه لتعيين الواحد قالوا ليس عدد أولى من عدد فينتفي العدد بالكلية كفي مسألة الوحدة فإنهم احتجوا على أن الإله واحد بنفي العدد بالكلية بهذه الحجة.
And let us now speak about the weakness of premises well-known among the people – i.e., the theologians – having many branches from great theological issues. The first: that when they wanted to negate an infinite number to affirm the One, they said: 'No number is more appropriate than another number,' so the number is negated entirely – as in the issue of unity, for they argued that God is One by negating number entirely with this argument.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage introduces a critique of a common argument used by theologians (mutakallimun). It is an objection-and-analysis passage. The author states they will discuss the weakness of certain well-known premises widely used in theology. The first example is the argument for God's unity (tawhid). Theologians, when wanting to deny an infinite number (i.e., any number other than one) to establish the oneness of God, argue that if there were multiple gods, no number would be more fitting than another, so no number at all should exist. Hence, they conclude that only one exists. The author implies this argument is weak, and the passage sets up a critical examination.

Key Terms
عدد غير متناه
الوحدة
الأولوية
المتكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the argument mentioned?
A) There is no number at all
B) Only one number is possible
C) Infinite number exists
D) Numbers are equally preferable

Key Takeaway
The 'no preference' argument is a classic kalam proof for God's unity, but it is considered weak because it can lead to absurd conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that God is One, is it because you have personally verified it through sound reasoning, or because you have accepted it from tradition? This passage shows that even a famous theological argument can be flawed. How does that challenge you to examine the foundations of your own belief?
?
The theologians used an argument that seemed logical but was weak. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on weak or unexamined arguments? What specific step will you take this week to strengthen your understanding of Tawhid?
?
If the 'No Preference' argument is flawed, what does that mean for your confidence in rational arguments for God's existence? How can you seek a firmer basis for your faith that does not rest on human reasoning alone?

The 'No Number is More Appropriate' Argument for Divine Oneness
Sanusi explains the mutakallimun's proof and clarifies key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument that positing a second god leads to an infinite regress, and why this proves the necessity of divine oneness.

Core Idea
If a second god existed, there is no reason to stop at two—so infinite gods follow, which is impossible.

This lesson presents a classical rational argument used by theologians to prove that God must be one. The argument hinges on the idea that if there were two gods, there would be no logical basis for preferring two over three, four, or any larger number, leading inevitably to an infinite number of deities—a conclusion that is rationally absurd. Sanusi explains the argument and clarifies important nuances about what 'infinite number' means in this context.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Observe the flow from the general premise to the specific application and then the clarifications.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main premise
Look for the statement 'No number is more appropriate than a number.' This is the core logical principle that drives the argument.
3
Understand the application to gods
See how the premise is applied: if a second god existed, it would not be more appropriate than a third or fourth, leading to an infinite number of gods, which is impossible (muhal). This proves that only one God can exist.
4
Note the clarification about 'infinite number'
Sanusi clarifies that 'infinite number' here means an infinite hierarchy of numbers (ranks), not an infinite number of units. This avoids a potential objection that the argument denies infinite numbers per se.
5
Appreciate the note on phrasing
Sanusi points out that the phrase 'if a second god were established' is a leniency; logically it should be 'if two were established,' but the meaning remains clear.
This shows attention to precise expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى أنهم إذا أرادوا نفي عدد غير متناه لتعيين الواحد قالوا ليس عدد أولى من عدد فينتفي العدد بالكلية كفي مسألة الوحدة فإنهم احتجوا على وحدانيته تعالى بأن الإله الواحد كاف في إيجاد الخلق فلو ثبت إله ثان لم يكن أولى الثالث والرابع هكذا فيلزم آلهة لا تتناهى وذلك محال فالقول بالعدد باطل لإفضائه إلى ذلك المحال. قوله : (نفي عدد غير متناه لم يرد به غير متناهي الآحاد حتى يرد أن المقصود نفي العدد بالكلية لا نفي ما لا يتناهى آحاده وإن نفي غير المتناهي ثابت بالبراهين فلا حاجة إلى نفيه بل أراد به غير متناه مراتبه يعني نفي العدد بجميع مراتبه وكذا في قوله أرادوا إثبات عدد غير متناه. قوله : (فلو ثبت إله ثان إلخ) المناسب للسياق فلو ثبت اثنان لم يكن أولى من ثلاثة وأربعة لأن الكلام في نفي مراتب الأعداد إلا أنه تسامح لاستلزام ثبوت الثاني والثالث والرابع ثبوت الاثنين والثلاثة والأربعة.
The first [premise]: when they want to deny an infinite number (ghayr mutanahi) to establish the One, they say: 'No number is more appropriate (awla) than a number,' so the number is entirely negated. As in the question of unity (al-wahda): they argued for His transcendence's oneness by saying: the one God suffices for creating creation; if a second god were established, it would not be more appropriate than a third and fourth — thus deities infinite (la tanahi) would follow, and that is impossible (muhal). So the statement of multiplicity is invalid because it leads to that impossibility. His saying: (denial of an infinite number) He did not mean infinite in units (mutanahi al-ahad) so that it might be objected that the intent is to deny number entirely, not to deny what is infinite in its units; and indeed the denial of the infinite is established by proofs, so there is no need to deny it. Rather, he meant by it infinite in its ranks, i.e., denial of number in all its ranks, and similarly in his saying: they wanted to prove an infinite number. His saying: (if a second god were established, etc.) What is appropriate to the context is: if two were established, they would not be more appropriate than three and four, because the discourse is about denying the ranks of numbers; but this is a leniency because the establishment of the second, third, and fourth entails the establishment of two, three, and four.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's famous argument from 'no number is more appropriate than a number' to disprove multiplicity of gods. The argument begins by stating that any claim of multiplicity (e.g., two gods) leads to an infinite regress because there is no reason to prefer any finite number over another; thus, the whole concept of number (beyond one) is negated. The specific application to divine unity: the one God is sufficient for creation; if a second god existed, there would be no rational ground to stop at two rather than three, four, or infinite, which is absurd. The commentary clarifies that the 'infinite number' here refers to an infinite hierarchy of numbers, not an infinite number of units, and that the denial of infinite numbers is independently proven. The commentator also notes that the phrasing 'if a second god were established' is somewhat loose; logically, it should be 'if two were established, they would not be more appropriate than three and four,' but the author omits 'two' for brevity, relying on the implication.

Key Terms
عدد غير متناه
ليس عدد أولى من عدد
إله ثان
وحدانية
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies infinite numbers in themselves, so it contradicts other proofs about the impossibility of an actual infinite.
Correction: The argument does not deny infinite numbers per se; it denies the infinite hierarchy of numbers (ranks) for the purpose of proving oneness. The denial of actual infinite is established by separate proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the argument presented, what is the consequence of claiming a second god exists?
A) It proves the second god is unnecessary.
B) It leads to an infinite chain of gods with no basis for stopping.
C) It establishes the equality of two gods.
D) It shows that one god is sufficient.

Key Takeaway
The 'no number is more appropriate' argument shows that any claim of multiple gods leads to an absurd infinite regress, thus establishing divine oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if there is only one God, or do you act as though there are multiple sources of power and control in your life—like wealth, status, or people's approval—that you treat as 'second gods'? What would it mean to let the logical impossibility of multiplicity dismantle each of those idols in your heart today?
?
This argument shows that any claim of multiple gods is irrational. When you face moments of doubt or negligence in affirming tawhid, how does this proof confront your own hesitation? What specific step can you take this week to deepen your intellectual conviction so that it transforms your daily actions?
?
The premise 'no number is more appropriate than a number' exposes the arbitrariness of stopping at any number other than one. Are there areas in your obedience where you 'stop at two'—giving Allah some worship but reserving part of your heart for something else? What will it cost you to surrender that remaining portion to the One who alone suffices?

The Tawhid Proof from the Impossibility of Infinite Gods
Al-Jurjani presents and explains the proof of God's oneness from the insufficiency of a second god.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sufficiency of one God for creation necessitates His oneness and why a second god would lead to an impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
One God suffices; a second leads to infinite absurdity.

This lesson presents a classic kalam proof for tawhid. Al-Jurjani argues that since one God is sufficient for all creation, admitting a second would open the door to an endless chain of gods, which is rationally impossible. This argument refutes any claim of multiple deities by showing its logical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation slowly, letting the logical flow sink in before analyzing details.
Notice the concise style; every phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the premise (one God suffices), the conditional (if a second, then no priority to stop), and the conclusion (infinite regress → impossible → multiplicity false).
3
Understand the reasoning about numbers
Al-Jurjani explains that the argument technically should start with 'if two were established' because the discussion is about ranks of numbers. The phrasing is a concession, but the meaning is preserved.
This clarifies a subtle logical point.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why an infinite regress of gods is impossible in Islamic theology—it would violate the principle of a necessary being.
This proof is foundational for tawhid.
5
Test your understanding
Try to restate the argument in your own words and answer the checkpoint question to confirm comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد السادس : في المقدمات
وحدانيته تعالى بأن الإله الواحد كاف في إيجاد الخلق فلو ثبت إله ثان لم يكن أولى الثالث والرابع ،هكذا فيلزم آلهة لا تتناهى وذلك ،محال، فالقول بالعدد باطل لإفضائه إلى ذلك المحال
قوله : (فلو ثبت إله ثان إلخ ) المناسب للسياق فلو ثبت اثنان لم يكن أولى من ثلاثة وأربعة، لأن الكلام في نفي مراتب الأعداد إلا أنه تسامح لاستلزام ثبوت الثاني والثالث والرابع ثبوت الاثنين والثلاثة والأربعة .
The sixth observation - the sixth purpose: on preliminary matters. His oneness is established by the fact that one God is sufficient for creation. If a second god were established, it would not be more fitting than a third and fourth, thus leading to infinite gods, and that is impossible. So the claim of multiplicity is false because it leads to that impossibility. His statement: 'If a second god were established, etc.' The context would require 'if two were established, it would not be more fitting than three and four,' because the discussion is about negating the ranks of numbers, but it is a concession because the establishment of the second, third, and fourth entails the establishment of two, three, and four.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for tawhid (God's oneness) based on the principle of no priority among numbers. It begins by stating that one God is sufficient for creation. If a second god were possible, there would be no reason to prefer two over three or four, leading to an infinite number of gods—an impossibility. Therefore, the claim of multiplicity is refuted. The commentator clarifies that the phrasing 'if a second god were established' is a concession; technically the argument should say 'if two were established' because the discussion concerns ranks of numbers. But the meaning is preserved because establishing the second, third, and fourth implies establishing two, three, and four. The argument assumes that numbers are homogenous in terms of possibility, so if one is possible, all are possible, leading to an infinite regress.

Key Terms
الإله (al-ilah)
التوحيد (al-tawhid)
العدد (al-'adad)
المحال (al-muhal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two gods exist, they could simply coexist without requiring a third, so the argument fails.
Correction: The argument is not about coexistence but about the logic of possibility. If two gods are possible, then three are equally possible because numbers have no inherent priority. This opens the door to infinite possibility, which is rationally impossible. Hence, the impossibility of infinite gods blocks the possibility of any multiplicity.

Checkpoint
What is the key assumption behind the proof that a second god would lead to infinite gods?
A) All numbers are equal in value.
B) There is no priority among numbers in possibility of existence.
C) God cannot create more than one world.
D) Humans cannot conceive of more than one god.

Key Takeaway
The oneness of God is established by the logical impossibility of infinite deities, rooted in the sufficiency of a single Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that one God is sufficient for all creation. In what areas of your life do you still act as if you need another 'helper' besides Him—wealth, status, relationships? How can you concretely entrust that area to His sufficiency today?
?
If the logical impossibility of a second god is absolute, what does it say about your heart when you seek approval or security from created beings as if they share His power? Repent and realign your worship.
?
The argument hinges on the principle that if a second were possible, no number could be ruled out. Are there 'small gods' in your life—idols of comfort, reputation, or control—that you have allowed because you think 'just one more' won't hurt? How will you demolish them starting now?

The Third Type of Demand: Proven by Reason and Transmission
Defines the third demand and gives examples of dual proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the third type of demand in kalam can be established by both rational and transmitted proofs, as exemplified by temporal origination and divine unity.

Core Idea
The third demand is independent of transmission yet provable by both reason and revelation.

Sanusi introduces the third category of theological demands: those that do not depend on transmission for their proof. Using the example of temporal origination, he shows how such a point can be rationally demonstrated through the possibility of the world, and then also confirmed by scriptural reports. This lesson explores that dual proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining the third demand
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author defines this category and gives the first example of temporal origination.
2
Identify the rational proof
The passage explains that the Maker can be proven without assuming temporal origination, using the argument from possibility (imkān). Find that argument in the text.
The rational proof does not depend on transmission.
3
See the transmitted proof
The passage then states that the same demand can also be proven by transmission, because transmission does not depend on it. Observe the example of tawḥīd being proven by scriptural proofs.
This shows the flexibility of the third demand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث من المطالب ما عداهما نحو الحدوث فإن صحة النقل غير متوقفة على حدوث العالم إذ يمكن إثبات الصانع دونه بأن يستدل على وجوده بإمكان العالم، ثم يثبت كونه عالماً ومرسلاً للرسل، ثم يثبت بإخبار الرسل حدوث العالم ونحو الوحدة فإن إرسال الرسل لا يتوقف على كون الإله واحداً، فجاز أن يثبت التوحيد بالأدلة السمعية فهذا المطلوب يمكن إثباته بالعقل، إذ يمتنع خلافه عقلاً بالدليل العقلي الدال عليه ويمكن أيضاً إثباته بالنقل لعدم توقفه عليه كما عرفت. قوله : (بإمكان العالم على ما هو طريقة المحققين من أن العالم ممكن موجود، وكل ممكن موجود لا بد له من فاعل واجب الوجود قطعاً للتسلسل دون الحدوث على ما هو طريقة جمهور المتكلمين. قوله : (ثم يثبت كونه عالماً إلخ) اكتفى ها هنا على كونه عالماً مع أنه لا بد من إثبات كونه قادراً مختاراً، إما للإحالة على ما ذكر سابقاً، فحينئذ لا بد من إثبات قدرته وإرادته بدليل لا يتوقف على حدوث العالم على ما قرره المصنف في بحث قدرته تعالى وإرادته تعالى، وإما للإشارة إلى أن التحقيق أن ثبوت الإرسال لا يتوقف إلا على وجود الصانع، وعلمه فإن الفلاسفة قائلون : بالإرسال مع قولهم بإيجابه تعالى وعندي أن الحق ما أفاده المصنف من توقف صحة النقل على العلم بوجود الصانع بالنبوة فقط، فإن الجهال في زمن النبي كانوا يصدقونه في دعوى النبوة بعد ظهور المعجزة مع عدم علمهم بكونه تعالى عالماً قادراً مختاراً، نعم إن ثبوت النبوة في نفس الأمر متوقف على ذلك وأما العلم فكلا. قوله : ( غير متوقفة على حدوث العالم فيه أن صحة النقل تتوقف على القدرة والاختيار
The third of the demands is what is other than them, such as temporal origination (al-ḥudūth). For the soundness of transmission is not dependent on the temporal origination of the world, since it is possible to prove the Maker without it, by inferring His existence from the possibility (imkān) of the world, then establishing that He is knowing and a sender of messengers, then establishing by the reports of the messengers the temporal origination of the world. And likewise oneness (al-waḥda), for the sending of messengers does not depend on God being one, so it is permissible to establish tawḥīd by scriptural proofs. So this demand can be established by reason, since its contrary is rationally impossible by a rational proof indicating it, and it can also be established by transmission because it does not depend on it, as you have learned. His statement: “by the possibility of the world, according to the method of the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn) that the world is possible and existent, and every possible existent must have an agent of necessary existence to stop infinite regress, not by temporal origination according to the method of the majority of theologians.” His statement: “then He is established as knowing, etc.” He sufficed here with His being knowing, although it is necessary to establish that He is powerful and willing—either by referring to what was mentioned previously, so then it is necessary to establish His power and will by a proof not dependent on the temporal origination of the world, as the author established in the discussion of His power and will; or to indicate that the verified view is that the establishment of sending (al-irsāl) does not depend except on the existence of the Maker and His knowledge. For the philosophers say that sending occurs with their statement that He necessitates, may He be exalted. And in my view, the truth is what the author conveyed: that the soundness of transmission depends on knowledge of the existence of the Maker through prophethood only. For the ignorant in the time of the Prophet used to believe him in his claim of prophethood after the appearance of the miracle, despite their lack of knowledge that He, may He be exalted, is knowing, powerful, and willing. Yes, the establishment of prophethood in itself depends on that, but as for knowledge, no. His statement: “is not dependent on the temporal origination of the world” means that the soundness of transmission depends on power and will.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of demand (matlab): matters other than the first two, exemplified by temporal origination (ḥudūth). The commentator argues that the soundness of transmission does not depend on the temporal origination of the world; rather, one can prove the existence of the Maker through the possibility (imkān) of the world, then establish His knowledge and that He sends messengers, and then the reports of the messengers can prove temporal origination. Similarly, divine unity (tawḥīd) can be proven by scriptural proofs because sending messengers does not require that God be one. Thus, this type of demand can be proven both rationally (since its contrary is impossible by a rational proof) and by transmission (since transmission does not depend on it). The commentator then elaborates on the method of proof: the 'method of the verifiers' uses possibility and the necessity of a necessary existent to stop infinite regress, unlike the majority of theologians who use temporal origination. He also discusses whether the proof of prophethood requires a full set of divine attributes, noting that ignorant people accepted the Prophet's prophethood after miracles without knowledge of all attributes. The passage concludes with a remark that the soundness of transmission depends on power and will, not on temporal origination.

Key Terms
الحدوث (al-ḥudūth: temporal origination)
الإمكان (al-imkān: possibility)
التوحيد (al-tawḥīd: divine unity)
النقل (al-naql: transmission)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the third type of demand be proven both rationally and by transmission?
A) Because it is a necessary truth
B) Because its rational proof does not depend on transmission
C) Because the Qur'an explicitly states it
D) Because it is a matter of faith

Key Takeaway
The third type of demand is unique because it can be proven by both rational argument and transmitted reports, without one depending on the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or listen to a sermon, do you ever pause to reflect on how reason and revelation both confirm the same truth? Or do you tend to separate them in your mind? How might your faith deepen if you intentionally looked for that harmony—starting from today?
?
The passage shows that you can prove some of the most important beliefs—like God's existence and unity—using either your intellect or the transmitted message of the prophets. Which one do you personally find more convincing? Why do you think that is? And if you lean on one at the expense of the other, what step will you take this week to engage with the other source?

Two Methods of Proof and the Attributes Required for Prophethood
Explains the two rational methods and the conditions for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main methods for proving the Maker (possibility vs. temporal origination) and the minimal conditions for accepting prophethood through transmission.

Core Idea
Prophethood can be accepted through miracles even without full knowledge of divine attributes.

In this passage, the commentator elaborates on the methods used to prove the Maker, contrasting the verifiers' method (based on possibility) with the majority's method (based on temporal origination). He then addresses what knowledge of God's attributes is required for transmission to be sound, using the example of ignorant people accepting the Prophet's claim after seeing a miracle.

Guided Path
1
Compare the two methods of proof
Read the part where the commentator explains 'his statement: by the possibility of the world...' and note how the verifiers use possibility to avoid infinite regress, unlike the majority who use temporal origination.
This is a key methodological difference in kalam.
2
Understand the discussion on divine attributes
The commentator discusses why the author only mentioned 'knowing' and not 'powerful' and 'willing'. He gives two possible reasons. Identify these two explanations.
3
Learn the example of the ignorant
The commentator uses the example of ignorant people who accepted the Prophet's prophethood after a miracle, despite not knowing that God is knowing, powerful, and willing. This shows that transmission can be sound even without full knowledge of attributes.
The soundness of transmission depends on knowledge of the Maker through prophethood only.

Objections to the Criterion: Necessary Existent and Sensory Particulars
Iji defends the criterion against two objections concerning divine simplicity and mental particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iji defends the criterion that external existents are set apart by both reality and personal identity by addressing objections related to the Necessary Existent and sensory particulars.

Core Idea
The Necessary Existent and sensory particulars challenge the criterion but are resolved by conceptual distinction.

In this passage, Iji addresses two potential counterexamples to his criterion that every external existent is set apart from others by both its essence (reality) and its individual identity (particularization). The first concerns Allah, who is external yet has no particularization distinct from His essence. The second concerns sensory particulars in the mind, which are distinct by both yet are not external. The answers refine our understanding of the criterion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage followed by the literal translation. Get a sense of the objection-response structure.
Notice the two objections introduced by 'ورد' and the answer beginning with 'وقد يجاب'.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection argues that the Necessary Existent (Allah) lacks a separate particularization from His essence, so He cannot be set apart by both. Reflect on why this is a problem.
The objection relies on the fact that Allah's essence is simple, without parts.
3
Identify the second objection
The second objection points to sensory particulars (images in the internal senses) that are distinct by both reality and identity but are mental, not external. This challenges the necessity of external existence.
Sensory particulars are imprinted in the internal powers (e.g., common sense, memory).
4
Analyze the answer to the first objection
The answer explains that in the Necessary Existent, 'reality' and 'particularization' are distinct only by conceptual consideration (i'tibar), not in external existence. Thus He is still set apart by both, albeit conceptually.
This preserves the criterion while maintaining divine simplicity.
5
Understand the resolution for sensory particulars
The commentary clarifies that 'external existent' means outside the perceptive powers. Sensory particulars exist in the senses, so they are not external. The criterion remains valid for true external existents.
The objection is defused by defining terms precisely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد ذلك بأن الواجب تعالى موجود خارجي، وليس له تشخص يغاير حقيقته حتى ينحاز بهما معا عن غيره، وبأن الجزئيات المدركة بالحواس المرتسمة في القوى الباطنة منحازة عن غيرها بالحقيقة والهوية معاً، وليست موجودات خارجية بل ذهنية، وقد يجاب بأن الواجب سبحانه شيء واحد في حد ذاته إلا أن ذلك الشيء يسمى حقيقة من حيث أن الواجب به هو هو، ويسمى تشخصاً من حيث أنه المميز له على وجه لا يمكن فرض الشركة معه فقد انحاز الواجب بحقيقة وهوية شخصية متغايرتين اعتباراً. قوله : (ورد) بأن الواجب تعالى إلخ) وكذا التعيينات فإنها موجودات خارجية عند الفلاسفة، وليس لها تشخصات تغاير حقائها كما يشار إليه في بحث التعين. قوله : ( وبأن الجزئيات المدركة بالحواس الكلام في تحققها الحسي لا الخارجي، إذ لا شبهة فيه فإن قلت : الجزئي المدرك بالحواس يصدق عليه في تحققه الحسي أنه موجود خارجي، لأنه عرض قائم بقوة جسمانية قلت : لو صح الوجود الخارجي بهذا الاعتبار لكان الصور الذهنية موجودات خارجية، وبالجملة المراد بالموجود الخارجي الموجود في الخارج عن المشاعر أي القوى الدراكة فلا إشكال.
This is objected to: (a) The Necessary Existent (al-wajib), exalted is He, is an external existent, yet He has no particularization (tashakhkhus) that is other than His reality (haqiqah) so that He would be set apart by both together from other things. (b) Sensory particulars (al-juz'iyyat al-mudrakah bi al-hawass) that are imprinted in the internal powers are set apart from other things by both reality and identity, yet they are not external existents but mental existents. And it may be answered: The Necessary Existent, glorified is He, is one thing in itself, but that thing is called 'reality' insofar as the existent is what it is by it, and is called 'particularization' insofar as it is the distinguisher for Him in a way that prevents the supposition of sharing with Him; so the Necesssary Existent is set apart by both a reality and a personal identity that are distinct by consideration (i'tibar). His statement: 'This is objected to: (a) The Necessary Existent, etc.' Likewise the 'determinations' (ta'yinat) are external existents according to the philosophers, yet they have no particularizations that are other than their realities, as will be indicated in the discussion of determination. His statement: '(b) And that sensory particulars' — the discussion concerns their sensory actualization, not external, for there is no doubt about that. If you say: The sensory particular, in its sensory actualization, is predicated as an external existent because it is an accident subsisting in a corporeal power, I say: If external existence were correct by this consideration, then mental images would be external existents. In short, by 'external existent' is meant what exists outside the senses, i.e., the perceptive powers, so there is no problem.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the criterion that external existents are set apart by both reality and personal identity. The first objection concerns the Necessary Existent (Allah): He is an external existent, yet He has no particularization distinct from His essence; thus He cannot be set apart by both. The second objection concerns sensory particulars (individual images) that are imprinted in the internal senses (like the common sense or memory): these are distinct from other things by both their reality and their identity, yet they are not external existents but mental existents. The answer to the first objection is that in the Necessary Existent, 'reality' and 'particularization' are distinct only by mental consideration (i'tibar): the same thing is called 'reality' from the aspect of being that by which He is what He is, and 'particularization' from the aspect of being what distinguishes Him from others. Thus He is set apart by both, albeit only conceptually. The answer to the second objection is clarified in the commentary: the discussion concerns sensory actualization, not external existence. Sensory particulars are indeed present in the senses, but 'external existence' means existence outside the perceptive powers altogether. If sensory existence in the mind were considered external, then all mental images would be external, which is false. Therefore, the criterion holds.

Key Terms
الواجب تعالى
التشخص
الحقيقة
الهوية الشخصية
الجزئيات المدركة بالحواس
القوى الباطنة
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Necessary Existent (Allah) has a distinct particularization (tashakhkhus) separate from His essence, like created beings.
Correction: In the Necessary Existent, reality and particularization are distinct only by consideration (i'tibar); they are not separate entities. The same simple essence is called 'reality' from one aspect and 'particularization' from another.

Checkpoint
How does the response answer the objection that the Necessary Existent (al-wajib) has no separate particularization from His reality?
A) By denying that He is an external existent.
B) By distinguishing between 'reality' and 'particularization' as two different things in Him.
C) By asserting that 'reality' and 'particularization' are distinct only by mental consideration, not in external existence.
D) By claiming that the Necessary Existent does have a separate particularization.

Key Takeaway
Iji defends his criterion by showing that apparent counterexamples (Allah and mental particulars) do not violate it when concepts are properly distinguished and terms defined.

Definition of External and Mental Existence
Al-Iji defines external and mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Iji's core distinction between external and mental existence based on individuation.

Core Idea
External existence has both essence and individuality; mental existence has only essence.

Al-Iji begins with a crisp definition: an external existent is distinguished from others by both its universal essence and a unique individuation, while a mental existent is distinguished only by its essence because the mind grasps universals. This foundational distinction underlies many debates in Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once; note the key terms 'موجود خارجي', 'موجود ذهني', 'ماهية', 'تشخص', and 'انحياز'.
2
Identify the two criteria for distinction
The passage states that external existents are distinguished by both universal essence and individuation (tashakhkhuṣ), while mental existents are distinguished only by universal essence.
The mind perceives only universals, so mental existents lack unique individuality.
3
Contrast external and mental existence
Apply the criteria to examples: a concrete horse (external) vs. the concept of horse (mental). The concrete horse has a unique identity; the concept is universal.
4
Reflect on the reason
Why does mental existence lack individuation? Because the mind abstracts universals. Consider how this shapes the definition.
This prepares for later objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شخصية ) يمتنع بها فرض اشتراكه بين كثيرين ) فهو الموجود الخارجي وإلا فهو الموجود الذهني ) فإن الذهن لا يدرك إلا أمراً كليا فالموجود فيه لا ينحاز عن غيره إلا بحسب الماهية الكلية بخلاف الموجود الخارجي، فإنه ينحاز عن غيره بماهية كلية وتشخص ورد ذلك بأن الواجب تعالى موجود خارجي، وليس له تشخص يغاير حقيقته حتى ينحاز بهما معا عن غيره، وبأن الجزئيات المدركة بالحواس المرتسمة في القوى الباطنة منحازة عن غيرها بالحقيقة والهوية معاً، وليست موجودات خارجية بل ذهنية، وقد يجاب بأن الواجب سبحانه شيء واحد في حد ذاته إلا أن ذلك الشيء يسمى حقيقة من حيث أن الواجب به هو هو، ويسمى تشخصاً من حيث أنه المميز له على وجه لا يمكن فرض الشركة معه فقد انحاز الواجب بحقيقة وهوية شخصية متغايرتين اعتباراً، قوله : ( ورد ذلك إلخ) يعني أن المستفاد المذكور أن كل موجود خارجي، فهو ممتاز في الحقيقة والهوية والموجود الذهني ممتاز بالحقيقة دون الهوية وكلا الحكمين باطلان . قوله : (بل) (ذهنية فإن الموجود الذهني عندهم ما حصل في القوى العالية، أو في القوى القاصرة في نفسها، أو في آلاتها على ما يسوق إليه دلائل الوجود الذهني، وبهذا ظهر فساد التعليل المذكور بقوله فإن الذهن لا يدرك إلا أمراً كلياً. قوله : (متغايرتين اعتباراً فالمراد بقوله : بهوية أعم من هوية متغايرة للحقيقة بالذات أو بالاعتبار. قوله : (إلا) بحسب الماهية الكلية قيل : الصور الذهنية تمتاز عن غيرها بماهية وتشخص عارض ذهني لأنها صور شخصية حالة في نفس شخصية فلها هوية شخصية ينحاز بها أيضاً، فلا يستقيم الحصر أجيب بأن الهوية إنما تطلق على التشخص الخارجي سواء حصل في المشاعر أيضا أم لا، ولو سلم عمومها في حد ذاتها فمرادنا ذلك بقرينة المقام فلا إشكال فتأمل. قوله: (ورد) بأن الواجب تعالى إلخ) وكذا التعيينات فإنها موجودات خارجية عند الفلاسفة، وليس لها تشخصات تغاير حقائها كما يشار إليه في بحث التعين.
A personal [individuality] by which supposing sharing among many is prevented: it is the external existent, otherwise it is the mental existent. For the mind only perceives a universal thing, so that which exists in it is not distinguished from others except according to the universal essence, unlike the external existent, for it is distinguished from others by a universal essence and an individuation. This is refuted by the fact that the Necessary Existent, exalted, is an external existent, and He has no individuation that differs from His essence such that He is distinguished by both together from others, and by the fact that the particulars perceived by the senses, imprinted in the internal faculties, are distinguished from others by both essence and identity, yet they are not external existences but mental. It may be answered that the Necessary Existent, glorified, is one thing in itself, except that that thing is called essence inasmuch as by it the necessary is what it is, and is called individuation inasmuch as it is the distinguisher for Him in a way that sharing with Him cannot be supposed; so the Necessary is distinguished by an essence and a personal identity that are different by consideration. His saying: (And this is refuted etc.) means that the aforementioned implication is that every external existent is distinguished in essence and identity, and the mental existent is distinguished in essence without identity, and both judgments are false. His saying: (Rather) mental: for the mental existent in their view is what occurs in the higher faculties, or in the lower faculties in themselves, or in their instruments according to what the proofs of mental existence lead to, and by this the corruption of the mentioned explanation by his saying 'for the mind only perceives a universal thing' becomes apparent. His saying: (different by consideration): what is meant by his saying 'with an identity' is more general than an identity different from the essence by essence or by consideration. His saying: (Except) according to the universal essence. It is said: mental forms are distinguished from others by an essence and an accidental mental individuation, because they are individual forms subsisting in an individual soul, so they have a personal identity by which they are also distinguished, thus the restriction is not sound. It is answered that 'identity' is only applied to external individuation, whether it also occurs in the senses or not, and if its generality is granted in itself, our intent is that by the context of the situation, so no problem: consider carefully. His saying: (It is refuted) by the fact that the Necessary Exalted etc., likewise determinative [beings] are external existences according to the philosophers, and they have no individuations that differ from their realities, as will be indicated in the discussion of determination.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, objection, and answer. It begins by defining the distinction between external and mental existence: an external existent is distinguished (yanḥāzu) by both a universal essence (māhiyyah kulliyyah) and an individuation (tashakhkhuṣ), while a mental existent is distinguished only by the universal essence because the mind only grasps universals. This definition is immediately challenged by two objections. The first objection: the Necessary Existent (God) is external, but He has no individuation separate from His essence; thus the definition fails. The answer: God is one in Himself, but from one perspective He is called 'essence' (ḥaqīqah) and from another 'individuation' (tashakhkhuṣ); the same reality functions as both, so the distinction is valid by conceptual consideration (iʿtibār). The commentary then explains that the term 'identity' (huwiyyah) here is broad, covering both real and conceptual difference. It also addresses a subsidiary point: mental images have their own accidental individuation, but the intended sense of 'identity' here is external individuation, even if it also occurs mentally. The passage then returns to the first objection, noting that determinative beings (taʿayyunāt) according to philosophers are external and lack distinct individuations.

Key Terms
موجود خارجي
موجود ذهني
ماهية
تشخص
انحياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes external existence from mental existence according to this passage?
A) External existence has both essence and individuality; mental only essence.
B) External existence has only essence; mental has both.
C) Both have both essence and individuality.
D) Neither has individuality.

Key Takeaway
External existents are distinguished by both essence and individuality; mental existents are distinguished by essence alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own existence is unique—no one shares exactly your individuality (tashakhkhuṣ). How does reflecting on this uniqueness deepen your awareness that Allah knows you personally, with all your private thoughts and deeds? What will you do today to live with that consciousness?
?
The mind only perceives universals, but you experience your life as a series of particular moments. Are you ever tempted to live robotically, acting on general principles without engaging your heart in the here and now? How can you ensure your next act of worship is not just a mental concept but a real, personal encounter with Allah?
?
If mental existence lacks individuation, your mere intellectual knowledge of Islam is universal—but your faith must be personal. In what area of your life have you settled for a 'head' understanding instead of a 'heart' conviction, and what is one specific step you will take to make that truth your own?

Allah's Existence

6 min
The First Objection and Its Answer
Al-Iji raises the first objection and gives its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the definition of external existence and how Al-Iji answers it using the concept of iʿtibār (consideration).

Core Idea
The Necessary Existent has no separate individuality, yet is external; the answer lies in conceptual distinction.

After defining external and mental existence, the text immediately raises a powerful objection: the Necessary Existent (God) is external but has no individuation separate from His essence. Additionally, sense particulars in the mind seem to be distinguished by both essence and identity, yet they are mental. Al-Iji's answer revolves around the idea of 'consideration' (iʿtibār): the same reality can be viewed as essence in one respect and individuation in another. This lesson unpacks the objection and its sophisticated resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'ورد ذلك' up to 'متغايرتين اعتباراً'. Identify the two points of the objection: the Necessary Existent and sensory particulars.
2
Understand the problem
The Necessary Existent has no individuation separate from His essence. How can He be distinguished by both? Also, mental images seem to have both essence and identity, contradicting the definition.
3
Read Al-Iji's answer
Note the key phrase 'متغايرتين اعتباراً': essence and individuation are the same reality but conceptually distinct. This resolves the issue for the Necessary Existent.
4
Reflect on the concept of iʿtibār
Consider how this answer preserves the definition: external existence requires both essence and individuation, but they need not be really separate; conceptual distinction suffices for God.
The second prong about sensory particulars is addressed later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شخصية ) يمتنع بها فرض اشتراكه بين كثيرين ) فهو الموجود الخارجي وإلا فهو الموجود الذهني ) فإن الذهن لا يدرك إلا أمراً كليا فالموجود فيه لا ينحاز عن غيره إلا بحسب الماهية الكلية بخلاف الموجود الخارجي، فإنه ينحاز عن غيره بماهية كلية وتشخص ورد ذلك بأن الواجب تعالى موجود خارجي، وليس له تشخص يغاير حقيقته حتى ينحاز بهما معا عن غيره، وبأن الجزئيات المدركة بالحواس المرتسمة في القوى الباطنة منحازة عن غيرها بالحقيقة والهوية معاً، وليست موجودات خارجية بل ذهنية، وقد يجاب بأن الواجب سبحانه شيء واحد في حد ذاته إلا أن ذلك الشيء يسمى حقيقة من حيث أن الواجب به هو هو، ويسمى تشخصاً من حيث أنه المميز له على وجه لا يمكن فرض الشركة معه فقد انحاز الواجب بحقيقة وهوية شخصية متغايرتين اعتباراً، قوله : ( ورد ذلك إلخ) يعني أن المستفاد المذكور أن كل موجود خارجي، فهو ممتاز في الحقيقة والهوية والموجود الذهني ممتاز بالحقيقة دون الهوية وكلا الحكمين باطلان . قوله : (بل) (ذهنية فإن الموجود الذهني عندهم ما حصل في القوى العالية، أو في القوى القاصرة في نفسها، أو في آلاتها على ما يسوق إليه دلائل الوجود الذهني، وبهذا ظهر فساد التعليل المذكور بقوله فإن الذهن لا يدرك إلا أمراً كلياً. قوله : (متغايرتين اعتباراً فالمراد بقوله : بهوية أعم من هوية متغايرة للحقيقة بالذات أو بالاعتبار. قوله : (إلا) بحسب الماهية الكلية قيل : الصور الذهنية تمتاز عن غيرها بماهية وتشخص عارض ذهني لأنها صور شخصية حالة في نفس شخصية فلها هوية شخصية ينحاز بها أيضاً، فلا يستقيم الحصر أجيب بأن الهوية إنما تطلق على التشخص الخارجي سواء حصل في المشاعر أيضا أم لا، ولو سلم عمومها في حد ذاتها فمرادنا ذلك بقرينة المقام فلا إشكال فتأمل. قوله: (ورد) بأن الواجب تعالى إلخ) وكذا التعيينات فإنها موجودات خارجية عند الفلاسفة، وليس لها تشخصات تغاير حقائها كما يشار إليه في بحث التعين.
A personal [individuality] by which supposing sharing among many is prevented: it is the external existent, otherwise it is the mental existent. For the mind only perceives a universal thing, so that which exists in it is not distinguished from others except according to the universal essence, unlike the external existent, for it is distinguished from others by a universal essence and an individuation. This is refuted by the fact that the Necessary Existent, exalted, is an external existent, and He has no individuation that differs from His essence such that He is distinguished by both together from others, and by the fact that the particulars perceived by the senses, imprinted in the internal faculties, are distinguished from others by both essence and identity, yet they are not external existences but mental. His saying: (Rather) mental: for the mental existent in their view is what occurs in the higher faculties, or in the lower faculties in themselves, or in their instruments according to what the proofs of mental existence lead to, and by this the corruption of the mentioned explanation by his saying 'for the mind only perceives a universal thing' becomes apparent. His saying: (It is refuted) by the fact that the Necessary Exalted etc., likewise determinative [beings] are external existences according to the philosophers, and they have no individuations that differ from their realities, as will be indicated in the discussion of determination.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That external existence always requires a real, separate individuation from the essence.

How does Al-Iji answer the objection that the Necessary Existent has no separate individuation?
A) He admits the definition is flawed.
B) He says the Necessary Existent is not an external existent.
C) He says essence and individuation are the same reality but conceptually distinct.
D) He says the objection does not apply.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between essence and individuation can be by conceptual consideration, not only by real separation, as in the case of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate Allah's unity (tawheed), do you ever find yourself inadvertently separating His essence from His attributes in your mind? How does the lesson's concept of 'consideration' (iʿtibār) challenge you to purify your understanding so that your worship is directed to the One Simple Reality, not a conceptual composite?
?
The objection came from an assumption that external existence requires real separation. In your own life, how often do you assume that things must be physically or really distinct to be real? Can you think of an area of your faith where you hold a misconception similar to the objector's, and what practical step can you take today to replace that assumption with the truth that conceptual distinctions in Allah do not diminish His oneness?
?
Al-Iji's answer relies on a fine conceptual point. Is your knowledge of Allah built on such precise distinctions, or is it vague and prone to error? What specific change in your study habits or du'a will you make to ensure you know Allah as He truly is, not as your limited mind assumes?

The Distinction of Mental Forms and the Meaning of Identity
Al-Iji addresses the objection that mental forms have individuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that mental forms also have individuation, and how Al-Iji resolves it by restricting the meaning of 'identity' (huwiyyah) to external individuation.

Core Idea
Mental forms have accidental individuation, but 'identity' in the definition refers to external individuation.

After the main answer, the text considers a further objection: mental forms (images) are individual and distinguished from others by both essence and an accidental mental individuation—so why does the definition claim they lack individuation? Al-Iji's response refines the term 'identity' (huwiyyah) to mean external individuation, not mental. This lesson clarifies that nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage from 'قوله : (إلا) بحسب الماهية الكلية قيل' up to 'فلا إشكال فتأمل'. Identify the challenge: mental forms have accidental individuation.
2
Understand the challenge
If mental forms have both essence and a personal identity, then the definition's claim that mental existents lack individuation seems false.
3
Read the answer
Note that 'identity' (huwiyyah) in the definition refers to external individuation, not mental accidental individuation. Even if the term is general, context specifies.
4
Reflect on the resolution
This preserves the definition by distinguishing between real external individuation and accidental mental 'individuation'.
Think about why accidental individuation is not sufficient to make something an external existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شخصية ) يمتنع بها فرض اشتراكه بين كثيرين ) فهو الموجود الخارجي وإلا فهو الموجود الذهني ) فإن الذهن لا يدرك إلا أمراً كليا فالموجود فيه لا ينحاز عن غيره إلا بحسب الماهية الكلية بخلاف الموجود الخارجي، فإنه ينحاز عن غيره بماهية كلية وتشخص ورد ذلك بأن الواجب تعالى موجود خارجي، وليس له تشخص يغاير حقيقته حتى ينحاز بهما معا عن غيره، وبأن الجزئيات المدركة بالحواس المرتسمة في القوى الباطنة منحازة عن غيرها بالحقيقة والهوية معاً، وليست موجودات خارجية بل ذهنية، وقد يجاب بأن الواجب سبحانه شيء واحد في حد ذاته إلا أن ذلك الشيء يسمى حقيقة من حيث أن الواجب به هو هو، ويسمى تشخصاً من حيث أنه المميز له على وجه لا يمكن فرض الشركة معه فقد انحاز الواجب بحقيقة وهوية شخصية متغايرتين اعتباراً، قوله : ( ورد ذلك إلخ) يعني أن المستفاد المذكور أن كل موجود خارجي، فهو ممتاز في الحقيقة والهوية والموجود الذهني ممتاز بالحقيقة دون الهوية وكلا الحكمين باطلان . قوله : (بل) (ذهنية فإن الموجود الذهني عندهم ما حصل في القوى العالية، أو في القوى القاصرة في نفسها، أو في آلاتها على ما يسوق إليه دلائل الوجود الذهني، وبهذا ظهر فساد التعليل المذكور بقوله فإن الذهن لا يدرك إلا أمراً كلياً. قوله : (متغايرتين اعتباراً فالمراد بقوله : بهوية أعم من هوية متغايرة للحقيقة بالذات أو بالاعتبار. قوله : (إلا) بحسب الماهية الكلية قيل : الصور الذهنية تمتاز عن غيرها بماهية وتشخص عارض ذهني لأنها صور شخصية حالة في نفس شخصية فلها هوية شخصية ينحاز بها أيضاً، فلا يستقيم الحصر أجيب بأن الهوية إنما تطلق على التشخص الخارجي سواء حصل في المشاعر أيضا أم لا، ولو سلم عمومها في حد ذاتها فمرادنا ذلك بقرينة المقام فلا إشكال فتأمل. قوله: (ورد) بأن الواجب تعالى إلخ) وكذا التعيينات فإنها موجودات خارجية عند الفلاسفة، وليس لها تشخصات تغاير حقائها كما يشار إليه في بحث التعين.

The Objection Extended to Determinative Beings
Al-Iji extends the objection to determinative beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the same objection about lack of separate individuation applies to determinative beings (taʿayyunāt) according to the philosophers.

Core Idea
Determinative beings are external but lack separate individuations, mirroring the case of God.

The text closes by noting that the first objection about lacking separate individuation also applies to determinative beings (taʿayyunāt) as understood by philosophers. These are external existences that have no individuations distinct from their realities. This brief note links the discussion to a broader philosophical context.

Guided Path
1
Read the sentence about determinative beings
Read the Arabic: 'وكذا التعيينات فإنها موجودات خارجية عند الفلاسفة، وليس لها تشخصات تغاير حقائها'.
2
Understand the extension
The same problem arises: determinative beings are external but lack separate individuations, so they too challenge the definition unless we use the same answer (conceptual distinction).
3
Connect to the discussion
Realize that this shows the definition's answer is general and not limited to the divine case.
The text refers to a later discussion for more detail.

His saying: (different by consideration): what is meant by his saying 'with an identity' is more general than an identity different from the essence by essence or by consideration. His saying: (Except) according to the universal essence. It is said: mental forms are distinguished from others by an essence and an accidental mental individuation, because they are individual forms subsisting in an individual soul, so they have a personal identity by which they are also distinguished, thus the restriction is not sound. It is answered that 'identity' is only applied to external individuation, whether it also occurs in the senses or not, and if its generality is granted in itself, our intent is that by the context of the situation, so no problem: consider carefully. His saying: (It is refuted) by the fact that the Necessary Exalted etc., likewise determinative [beings] are external existences according to the philosophers, and they have no individuations that differ from their realities, as will be indicated in the discussion of determination.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, objection, and answer. It begins by defining the distinction between external and mental existence: an external existent is distinguished (yanḥāzu) by both a universal essence (māhiyyah kulliyyah) and an individuation (tashakhkhuṣ), while a mental existent is distinguished only by the universal essence because the mind only grasps universals. This definition is immediately challenged by two objections. The first objection: the Necessary Existent (God) is external, but He has no individuation separate from His essence; thus the definition fails. The answer: God is one in Himself, but from one perspective He is called 'essence' (ḥaqīqah) and from another 'individuation' (tashakhkhuṣ); the same reality functions as both, so the distinction is valid by conceptual consideration (iʿtibār). The commentary then explains that the term 'identity' (huwiyyah) here is broad, covering both real and conceptual difference. It also addresses a subsidiary point: mental images have their own accidental individuation, but the intended sense of 'identity' here is external individuation, even if it also occurs mentally. The passage then returns to the first objection, noting that determinative beings (taʿayyunāt) according to philosophers are external and lack distinct individuations.

Determinative beings are external, yet lack separate individuations, echoing the case of the Necessary Existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If determinative beings (like your own specific identity) have no individuation separate from their reality, what does that mean for your understanding of your own self as a creation of Allah? Are you simply an instance of an essence, or is your existence entirely dependent on Him at every moment? How does this realization change the way you view your daily actions and your need for Allah's constant sustenance?
?
The objection in this passage extends to determinative beings to show that existence without separate individuation is possible. How does this logical point confront any subtle pride you might have about your own unique identity? If even your 'determinateness' is not an independent reality, how can you take credit for anything about yourself? Let this lesson push you to attribute all your defining characteristics to Allah alone.

The First Argument and Its Refutation
First argument against the simple substance and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that a simple substance would necessitate composition in the Creator, and the refutation that sharing a descriptive attribute does not entail composition.

Core Idea
Sharing a negative attribute does not force composition in the Creator.

The author begins by noting that the existence of a simple substance (neither a body nor in a body) is merely possible due to lack of proof. He then presents a strong objection: if such a substance existed, it would share with the Creator a key description, requiring a distinguishing factor and leading to composition in the Creator, which is impossible. The commentator carefully refutes this, arguing that sharing a description—especially a negative one—does not imply composition, and that the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without dissecting it. Then read the translation. Let the argument and its structure come through before you analyze.
Notice the dense style of kalam reasoning.
2
Identify the first argument
Find where the author states that a simple substance would share the description 'not a body nor in a body' with the Creator, leading to composition (tarkib). Understand the logical chain.
The argument is: shared description → need for distinguisher → composition → impossible.
3
Understand the refutation
The commentator argues that sharing a descriptive attribute does not necessitate composition, especially when the attribute is negative. He also addresses an objection about negative distinguishing factors.
Key point: the shared attribute is negative and does not constitute a part of the essence.

The Second Stand: On Universal Matters. We say) since we found no proof for it, it is possible that it exists and possible that it does not exist, whether it is possible or impossible. Some are content with this extent, namely that its existence is not established. Some affirm its impossibility for two reasons: First, that if it existed, it would share with the Creator this description) namely that it is not a body nor in a body. Then the Creator must be distinguished from it by something else, i.e., other than this shared description, so composition (tarkib) in the Creator from the shared and the distinguishing would follow, and that is impossible. Second, that this description is the most specific attribute of the Creator, for if someone asks about Him, that questioner is answered only with it, i.e., with this description, so it is said: He is an existent not a body nor in a body. So if another shared in it, it would also share in the reality, and then either the contingent would be eternal or the eternal would be contingent. The answer to the first: that sharing in a description does not necessitate that. Specifically, his statement: (if it existed, etc.) Its summary: the existence of the simple substance (al-jawhar al-mujarrad) necessitates the Creator's sharing with it in being a simple essence, so the objection is not made by His attributes. His statement: (and that is impossible because it would necessitate the multiplicity of the necessary or its possibility or impossibility, because the part is either necessary in itself or possible or impossible, so composition in the Necessary is absolutely impossible. Yes, if it were said that the mental parts are purely abstract, the necessary consequence would only be the possibility of the Necessary in mental existence, but that is contrary to verification, and it is not harmful because those are not parts in reality due to the lack of constitution by them, so it does not contradict existence. His statement: (the most specific attribute of the Creator) the superlative form is derived from absolute specificity including real and relative, so it leads to its being a real specific property, and what is meant is that nothing is more specific than it, so it does not contradict the existence of an equal. His statement: (so composition would follow) it is said: why do they not allow that it be distinguished by a negative accident as is their doctrine? And it is negative) like the description under discussion. And) the answer to the second (is that we do not accept that this description is the most specific attribute of Him, but rather His most specific attributes are either essential existence (al-wujud al-dhati) or being the giver of existence to everything else or eternity, since no other shares in them. And) another answer to the second (is that this claim, i.e., the claim that this description is His most specific attribute, is not free from circularity, because its being His most specific attribute is only established if it is established that there is no contingent existent that is not a body nor in a body, so the premise of the proof depends on establishing the claim, so proving it by it is a circle. His statement: (since no other shares in them, and the attributes are not other than the essence) this proof implies that all of them are the most specific attributes, so the alternation in the text is because each one is sufficient as a basis for the denial. As for His attributes, even if they are eternal, they are not other than the essence, and if essential eternity (al-qidam al-dhati) were intended, the question about the attributes would not be relevant at all. His statement: (proving it by it is a circle) in it, he did not establish that it is the most specific attribute of the Creator by the claim, but by its occurrence in the answer to the question about His essence, so there is no circle, unless it is said that the claim of its occurrence in the answer is like the claim that there is no possible simple in existence, and in his statement 'not free' there is an indication that it is not free from weakness. His statement: (we do not accept that it is His most specific attribute) and his statement: (for if someone asks about Him, only it is given) is denied, and even if it is accepted, the answer with the more general necessarily entails distinction in general, and that is sufficient as is the way of the ancients, especially since equality is not established unless the correctness of the answer is established, and mere answer does not imply correctness. His statement: (since no other shares in them and the attributes are not other than Him) moreover, essential eternity, meaning independence of others, is without doubt exclusive to Him. His statement: (is not free from circularity because its being the most specific, etc.) in it there is discussion because its being the most specific attribute of God, even if it is accepted that in itself it depends on there being no existent that is not a body nor in a body, knowledge does not depend on knowledge of that such that circularity follows, since it is possible to infer that specificity from its occurrence in the answer; if the validity of the inference is denied, then the matter reverts to the first argument in the outcome.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents a theological discussion about the existence of a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) that is neither a body nor a inhering in a body. It begins by noting that such a substance is possible in the absence of proof against it. Some scholars are content to say its existence is unproven, while others affirm its impossibility for two reasons.

The first reason: if such a substance existed, it would share with the Creator the attribute of being neither a body nor in a body. To distinguish the Creator from this substance, an additional distinguishing factor would be needed, leading to composition (tarkib) in the Creator, which is impossible. The commentator explains that composition in the necessary being is impossible because it would entail multiplicity, possibility, or impossibility of the necessary. However, if the parts are merely mental abstractions, the composition is not real and does not affect existence. He refutes the objection that the distinguishing factor could be a negative attribute, arguing that if the shared attribute is negative, the possible simple would also share it, so it does not distinguish.

The second reason: the description of being neither a body nor in a body is claimed to be the most specific attribute of the Creator, because whenever anyone asks about God, the answer is given with this description. If another being shared this attribute, it would share in the divine reality, leading to an impossible consequence: either the contingent would be eternal or the eternal would be contingent.

The commentator then provides answers to both arguments. For the first, he argues that sharing a descriptive attribute does not necessitate composition; the Creator's attributes are not other than His essence, so the objection does not apply. For the second, he gives multiple responses: first, that we do not accept this description as the most specific attribute; the most specific attributes could be essential existence, being the giver of existence, or eternity, which are not shared. Second, the claim of specificity is circular because it assumes the non-existence of a contingent simple substance, which is exactly what is in question. Further commentary discusses whether the answer with this description proves exclusivity, and whether the argument involves a vicious circle.

The passage concludes with additional objections and refinements, including the point that sharing in quiddity does not necessitate sharing in eternity or contingency, as the author will explain later.

Key Terms
الجوهر المجرد
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sharing a negative attribute necessarily implies composition in the Creator.
Correction: The commentator shows that negative attributes can be shared without composition because they do not constitute parts of the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument given for the impossibility of the simple substance?
A) It would lead to the Creator having a body.
B) It would necessitate composition in the Creator.
C) It would make the Creator dependent on others.
D) It would contradict divine unity.

Key Takeaway
The first argument fails because sharing a descriptive negative attribute does not necessitate composition in the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's uniqueness, do you often rely on negations (e.g., 'He is not like anything') without actively affirming His positive attributes? How might this intellectual shortcut affect the depth of your worship and awe? What can you do today to move beyond mere negation to a more vibrant, positive knowledge of God?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly logical argument against God's simplicity fails if we misinterpret negative attributes. Have you ever unwittingly restricted God's majesty by imposing human categories onto Him—thinking, for example, that He must have a location or be composed of parts? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your understanding of God, and what practical step will you take to guard your heart from such subtle errors?

Allah's Existence
intermediate

The Second Argument and Its Refutation
Second argument against the simple substance and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that the description 'not a body nor in a body' is the most specific attribute of the Creator, and the refutations that it is not exclusive and that the argument is circular.

Core Idea
Exclusivity of a descriptive attribute is not established and the argument involves circular reasoning.

The second argument claims that the attribute of being neither a body nor in a body is the most specific attribute of the Creator, because that is the only answer given when asked about God. Therefore, no other being can share it, otherwise the contingent would be eternal or the eternal contingent. The commentator offers multiple refutations: the most specific attributes are essential existence, giving existence, or eternity; the claim of exclusivity is circular because it assumes the non-existence of a possible simple substance, which is the very point in question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second argument
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'قوله : (أخص صفات الباري...' through the translation. Notice how the author presents the argument about the most specific attribute.
The argument hinges on the claim that this description is exclusively used in answering the question about God.
2
Identify the refutations
The commentator gives two main responses: first, that the most specific attributes are different (essential existence, giving existence, eternity); second, that the argument is circular because it assumes what it tries to prove.
The circularity point is subtle: to claim the description is most specific already assumes no other being shares it.
3
Understand the follow-up discussion
The passage also discusses whether the answer with this description proves exclusivity, and whether the circle is vicious. Note the mention of the Karramiyya and the distinction between quiddity and attributes.
The commentator also notes that sharing in quiddity does not necessitate sharing in eternity or contingency.

The Second Stand: On Universal Matters. We say) since we found no proof for it, it is possible that it exists and possible that it does not exist, whether it is possible or impossible. Some are content with this extent, namely that its existence is not established. Some affirm its impossibility for two reasons: First, that if it existed, it would share with the Creator this description) namely that it is not a body nor in a body.
