Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents a theological discussion about the existence of a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) that is neither a body nor a inhering in a body. It begins by noting that such a substance is possible in the absence of proof against it. Some scholars are content to say its existence is unproven, while others affirm its impossibility for two reasons.

The first reason: if such a substance existed, it would share with the Creator the attribute of being neither a body nor in a body. To distinguish the Creator from this substance, an additional distinguishing factor would be needed, leading to composition (tarkib) in the Creator, which is impossible. The commentator explains that composition in the necessary being is impossible because it would entail multiplicity, possibility, or impossibility of the necessary. However, if the parts are merely mental abstractions, the composition is not real and does not affect existence. He refutes the objection that the distinguishing factor could be a negative attribute, arguing that if the shared attribute is negative, the possible simple would also share it, so it does not distinguish.

The second reason: the description of being neither a body nor in a body is claimed to be the most specific attribute of the Creator, because whenever anyone asks about God, the answer is given with this description. If another being shared this attribute, it would share in the divine reality, leading to an impossible consequence: either the contingent would be eternal or the eternal would be contingent.

The commentator then provides answers to both arguments. For the first, he argues that sharing a descriptive attribute does not necessitate composition; the Creator's attributes are not other than His essence, so the objection does not apply. For the second, he gives multiple responses: first, that we do not accept this description as the most specific attribute; the most specific attributes could be essential existence, being the giver of existence, or eternity, which are not shared. Second, the claim of specificity is circular because it assumes the non-existence of a contingent simple substance, which is exactly what is in question. Further commentary discusses whether the answer with this description proves exclusivity, and whether the argument involves a vicious circle.

The passage concludes with additional objections and refinements, including the point that sharing in quiddity does not necessitate sharing in eternity or contingency, as the author will explain later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument from exclusive answer proves the impossibility of a simple substance.
Correction: The commentator shows that the exclusivity of the answer is not established, and the argument is circular.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the circularity objection in the second argument?
A) The argument assumes that the simple substance does not exist, which is exactly what needs proof.
B) The argument uses the description as both premise and conclusion.
C) The argument relies on a false assumption about divine attributes.
D) The argument is based on a misunderstanding of the term 'simple substance'.

Key Takeaway
The second argument is refuted because the description is not proven to be the most specific, and the argument involves circular reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your understanding of God, do you ever assume that a common description or title of God is exclusive to Him without solid proof? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of such assumptions? How can you actively verify your beliefs about God's attributes?
?
The refutation points out that the argument is circular—it assumes what it tries to prove. In your own life, are there beliefs about God or religion that you hold without realizing they are based on circular reasoning? What step will you take today to trace the foundations of your faith?
?
The Karramiyya thought God’s attributes were created and separate from His essence. Have you ever subtly thought of God’s attributes as independent entities rather than aspects of His one essence? How does this lesson call you to deepen your tawhid and see His attributes as inseparable from His being?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
8 min
The Self-Evidence of Existence: Three Opinions
Sanusi enumerates three opinions on existence's self-evidence and argues for the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on whether the concept of existence is self-evident or acquired, and why the preferred view is that it is self-evident.

Core Idea
The concept of existence is self-evident, but its self-evidence can still be argued for.

This passage from al-Mawaqif addresses a foundational question in metaphysics: is the concept of existence something we grasp immediately, or does it require definition? Three opinions are presented, and the author defends the first. Understanding this debate clarifies how theologians approach the most basic concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the opening sentences to find the three views on existence's self-evidence. Notice how each is stated concisely.
The Arabic uses 'فقيل' and 'وقيل' to introduce opinions.
2
Understand the chosen view
The author chooses the first opinion. See how he asserts that existence is self-evident (badihi).
The phrase 'والمختار أنه بديهي' indicates the chosen view.
3
Examine the argument
The passage then explains that even self-evident concepts can be argued for, because self-evidence is an external attribute. Pay attention to the reasoning.
4
Contrast with a misunderstanding
Note that the passage guards against the idea that self-evident things cannot be proven. The commentary clarifies that the attribute of self-evidence itself can be demonstrated.

The first observation: on existence and non-existence, containing aims. The first aim: on its definition. It is conceived, i.e., the definition of existence. It is said that it is self-evident (badihi) in its conception, so it cannot then be defined except by a verbal definition. And it is said that it is acquired (kasbi), so then its definition is necessary. And it is said: no, not at all, neither self-evident nor acquired. And the chosen view is that it is self-evident. (The aspects) and these aspects are either arguments, as is apparent from them, for the self-evidence (badahah) of a concept is an attribute external to it, so it is permissible that it be sought for it by demonstration (burhan). His saying: (The first) regarding its definition, i.e., whether it has a definition or not, and the second is either due to its self-evidence and the impossibility of conceiving it, so it is correct to interpret it by his saying: it is said, etc. And it is not objected what is said: that in this aim the definition of existence is not intended per se, making it a suspicious heading. His saying: (as is apparent from them) by the evidence of raising objections against them and answering them, for raising an objection to the alert and answering it has little benefit. His saying: (for the self-evidence of the concept, etc.) is a proof for an implied claim, meaning it is permissible to argue for self-evidence by [knowing] the essence, because its self-evidence is neither the same as its quiddity nor a part of it, such that its attribution to it after conceiving it by essence would be self-evident, according to what is established that knowledge of the attribution of the essential (dhati) to a thing after conceiving it by essence and paying attention is self-evident. Rather, it is external to it, so it is permissible that its attribution to it be theoretical (nazari). And this aspect is not... and what they said: regarding the self-evident, it is permissible that the judgment of its self-evidence be theoretical, based on the fact that its occurrence, since it was not by acquisition, oversight occurs about its occurrence initially. So if one intends knowledge of how it occurs, it needs reflection, unlike the theoretical, for it occurs by acquisition and difficulty, and oversight does not occur about how it occurs, so rarely is the judgment of the theoreticality of the theoretical in need of reflection, and it has no face. His saying: (the first in its definition, i.e., does it have a definition or not, and if it has a definition, what is it. His saying: (as is apparent from them) i.e., from the expression of aspects, for the apparent meaning of the lam is for causation, and we have explained in the discussions of knowledge the manner of need for knowledge of the self-evidence of the self-evident for reflection and its non-attainment by intuition, so remember. His saying: (for the self-evidence of the concept is an attribute external to it) and if it is conceded that it is internal, the self-evidence of the occurrence of the concept does not entail the self-evidence of knowledge of itself, nor of its parts, as preceded in the discussion of knowledge, but upon exit the matter becomes clear. So this restriction is clarification, not exclusion. Indeed, if the conception of existence by essence occurred, the attribution of self-evidence to it, on the assumption of its inclusion in it, would not be sought by demonstration, based on what is established that the attribution of an essential of a thing to it is not reasoned, but that occurrence has not been established.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage begins by classifying the opinions on whether the concept of existence is self-evident (badihi) or acquired (kasbi). The first opinion says it is self-evident, so it can only be defined verbally. The second says it is acquired, so it requires a definition. The third denies both. The author chooses the first, that existence is self-evident in conception. The passage then explains that the 'aspects' (wujuh) mentioned are arguments for self-evidence, and the reasoning is given: self-evidence is an attribute external to the concept, so it can be proven by demonstration. The commentary elaborates: the self-evidence of existence is not part of its essence, so proving that it is self-evident is a theoretical matter but does not contradict its being self-evident in itself. The passage also discusses objections and the need for reflection even on self-evident matters for some minds.

Key Terms
وجود
بديهي
كسبي
تصور
بداهة
برهان
ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident concepts cannot be argued for or proven.
Correction: The attribute of self-evidence itself can be proven theoretically, as shown in the passage.

Checkpoint
How many opinions are mentioned regarding whether the concept of existence is self-evident?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The concept of existence is self-evident, but its self-evidence can be established through demonstration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in the existence of God, but have you ever seriously examined why you consider that belief self-evident? Are you willing to investigate the grounds of your certainty today, even if it means facing doubt?
?
The passage argues that self-evidence does not mean it is known to all. Is there an area of your faith that you assume everyone should immediately see as true, but you have not personally verified? Will you this week seek to understand it more deeply?
?
If the self-evidence of existence can be proven, then your knowledge of God's existence is not merely inherited but can be rationally affirmed. Are you content to rely on blind acceptance, or will you strive to make that knowledge your own through reflection?

The First Note: Existence as Part of the Existent
Sanusi argues that existence is part of the existent and proves its self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that existence is a part of the existent and why this implies that existence is self-evident.

Core Idea
Existence is self-evident because it is a part of everything we conceive.

This lesson unpacks the first of several alerts (tanbihat) concerning the self-evidence of existence. The author argues that existence (wujud) is an integral part of the existent (mawjud) and, being a part of something self-evident, must itself be self-evident. We will follow the logical steps of this argument, including both the direct proof and the proof on concession.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Recognize the thesis: existence is a part of the existent. The author asserts this as an immediate conceptual truth.
This is based on the idea that the absolute is part of the restricted.
2
Follow the direct argument
Trace the reasoning: the absolute is part of the restricted; the conception of existence is self-evident (even children conceive it); therefore, the part of something self-evident is self-evident.
If existence were acquired, the whole (the existent) would also need definition, which is false.
3
Examine the concession argument
Suppose existence is not self-evident. Then a proof is needed. That proof's own existence must be self-evident to avoid infinite regress. Since existence is part of that proof's existence, it too is self-evident.
This argument relies on the necessity of a self-evident foundation.
4
Reflect on the objection and resolution
The commentary addresses an objection about the necessity of the proof's existence. Understand the distinction between necessity of assent and necessity of conception.
The author follows Imam al-Razi's method.

Alerts based on what was said that the judgment of the self-evidence of its conception is also self-evident, but the self-evident matters may need an alert for deficient minds. [The first] that it is a part of existence (juz’ wujudi) because the absolute is a part of the restricted necessarily, and it is conceived by self-evidence because one who cannot acquire, even the mentally disabled and children, conceives his existence definitely, and a part of what is conceived by self-evidence is self-evident. If it were acquired needing definition, then that [whole] conceived would also need that definition and thus would not be self-evident. And on the concession (wa ‘ala al-tanazzul) i.e., we concede that existence is not conceived by self-evidence and we say its conception is acquired, then one must end at a proof (dalil), i.e., a path leading, from whose existence follows the existence of the implied (madlul) which is the conception of existence, and its existence, i.e., the existence of that proof, must be necessary (daruri) to prevent infinite regress or circularity entailed by knowledge of the existence of every proof being derived from another proof. And thereby the proof for the self-evidence of the conception of existence is completed, for if the existence of that proof is conceived by self-evidence, then the absolute existence which is part of its existence is also self-evident. Imam al-Razi said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: knowledge... His saying: (to the deficient minds) i.e., those that cannot conceive their limits according to what is the basis of judgment. His saying: (needing that definition) with a need that is the same need of the part in reality, even if it differs in concept and relation to the part and whole, not with a self-subsistent need different from the need of the part such that the objection would be that we do not affirm another need for the whole, and subordinate such that it would be objected that it does not entail the theoreticality of the whole because the theoretical is what needs reflection per se, not per accidens. His saying: then one must end (etc.) i.e., one must acquire it by a necessary proof or end at it, but the first is omitted for brevity due to its clarity. His saying: (from whose existence follows its existence) i.e., from knowledge of its existence, for if it were not existent in reality, how would it entail the existence of the implied in reality? I mean its being realized in it, and if its existence were not known, how could it be used as a proof? Thus this point should be edited to match what will come in the answer. His saying: (and its existence) i.e., knowledge of its existence. His saying: (and its existence must be necessary to prevent infinite regress) it is said: if he means by the necessity of its existence the necessity of assent to it, then there is that the necessity of assent does not entail the necessity of the parts, for if he claimed its occurrence in children so that the necessity of its parts also follows, that is denied, and the discourse is not completed then merely by the necessity of this assent. And if he means the necessity of the conception of existence, it is far and not necessary. It is answered that the discourse is from the Imam and he has proceeded here according to his known method of arguing for the self-evidence of the parts by the self-evidence of assent, even if it is weak. Then it is possible to choose either of the two sides, but the first is more apparent.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a 'tanbih' (alert) that builds on the earlier claim that the judgment of self-evidence of existence is itself self-evident, but may need pointing out to deficient minds. The first alert argues that existence (wujud) is a part of the existent (mawjud). The reasoning: the absolute is necessarily part of the restricted; the conception of existence is self-evident because even those without acquisition (like the mentally disabled and children) conceive it; and a part of something self-evident is itself self-evident. If existence were acquired, then the whole (the existent) would also need definition, contradicting its self-evidence. Then, on the concession that existence is not self-evident, the argument proceeds to require a proof whose existence must be necessary to avoid infinite regress or circularity. This proof's existence itself involves existence as a part, so if that proof's existence is self-evident, then existence as a part is self-evident. The commentary clarifies aspects of need, the nature of 'necessity', and addresses an objection about the necessity of the proof's existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the self-evidence of a part does not imply the self-evidence of the whole; here the argument is that if the whole (the existent) is self-evident, then its part (existence) must also be self-evident.
Correction: The passage argues the opposite direction: because existence is a part of something self-evident, it is self-evident itself. The self-evidence of the whole entails the self-evidence of its part.

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of existence is established either directly (as part of the self-evident existent) or indirectly (by requiring a self-evident proof to avoid regression).

?
When you build your faith on proofs, do you ever stop to check whether those proofs themselves rest on something self-evident? If not, you are building on an infinite regress. Will you today accept the self-evidence of existence as the bedrock of your certainty in the Creator?

Al-Razi's Proof for the Self-Evidence of Existence
Al-Razi's proof for self-evidence of existence, with objection and reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that man's knowledge of his own existence is not acquired and how this establishes the self-evidence of the concept of existence.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge makes existence self-evident.

Imam al-Razi presents a concise logical proof that the human knowledge of one's own existence is not acquired. He argues that existence is a part of one's existence, and since knowledge of the whole is not acquired, the knowledge of the part is even more clearly non-acquired. In this lesson, we examine his argument and his response to a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the first paragraph
Read al-Razi's statement from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya. Note the main claim that knowledge of existence is not acquired.
Focus on the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
2
Identify the proof structure
Break down the argument: premise 1 (knowledge of existence is non-acquired? No, that's the conclusion). Actually, al-Razi states the conclusion first and then gives a syllogism: existence is part of his existence, knowledge of part precedes whole, and what precedes the non-acquired is more non-acquired.
The proof uses the relation of part to whole.
3
Examine the objection and reply
Al-Razi anticipates an objection: perhaps knowledge of existence is acquired. His reply has two parts: (1) this will be disproven later, and (2) even if accepted, it doesn't harm the purpose because we must have a self-evident foundation.
The second reply introduces the regress argument.
4
Connect to your own experience
Reflect: Is your knowledge that you exist something you had to learn or think about? Al-Razi says it's immediate, like for children.
This makes the abstract argument concrete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية : علم الإنسان بوجود نفسه غير مكتسب، والوجود جزء من وجوده والعلم بالجزء سابق على العلم بالكل، والسابق على غير المكتسب أولى بأن لا يكون مكتسباً، فإن قيل: علم الإنسان بوجوده مكتسب قلنا سنبطله في باب النفس وبتقدير التسليم لا قدح في المقصود لأنا ما لم نعرف وجود الدليل لا يمكننا أن نستدل به على وجود المدلول وليس العلم بوجود كل دليل محتاجاً إلى دليل آخر، بل لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل يكون العلم بوجوده بديهياً، فكذا العلم بالوجود المطلق فإذا حمل كلامه هذا على أن علم كل إنسان بأنه موجود ضروري فلا إشكال في ذكر الدليل حمل على أن كل إنسان يتصور وجوده بديهة فالمراد من الدليل هو الطريق الموصل إلى التصور كما أشرنا إليه، ثم إن المصنف مع تصريحه بأن وجودي متصور بالبديهة، وجزء المتصور
قوله : ( بوجوده ) أي بأنا موجود فيكون تعبيراً عن القضية بمضمونه الذي هو مفهوم وجودي، أو بالوجود المقيد قوله : ( غير مكتسب) أي لا يحتاج إلى الاكتساب أصلاً لكونه حاصلاً للبله والصبيان . قوله : ( والوجود أي المطلق جزء من وجوده أي من القضية التي عبر عنها بوجود نفسه، لكونه محمولاً فيها أو من الوجود المقيد لأن المطلق جزء المقيد . قوله : ( على غير المكتسب) أي القضية التي لا تحتاج إلى الاكتساب أصلاً، أو المتصور الذي كذلك.
قوله : ( بوجوده ) أي بأنا موجود أو الوجود المقيد .
قوله: (فإذا حمل إلخ) قد عرفت طريق الحمل عليه، ولا يلزم حينئذ رجوع الوجه الأول إلى الثاني على ما وهم بل الشركة بينهما في كون الاستدلال ببداهة الكل على بداهة الجزء لكن الكل والجزء فيهما مختلفان .
قوله: (فلا إشكال إلخ فإن قلت قد مر أن المراد بغير المكتسب ما لا يحتاج إلى الكسب أصلاً، فيجوز أن يكون احتياج العلم بأنا موجود باعتبار طرفه، فلا يلزم الاحتياج إلى الدليل بالمعنى المتعارف، فالإشكال في ذكر الدليل في هذا الحمل أيضاً ثابت، قلت : قد عرفت في تقسيم الطريق الموصل أن الدليل عبارة عن الطريق الموصل إلى المطلوب التصديقي، وهذا التعريف صادق على ما يفيد العلم بأنا موجود سواء كان اكتسابه منه من حيث الحكم أو من حيث الطرف، وأما لزوم أن لا يكون للمطلوب التصديقي طريق منفرد عن المطلوب التصوري فهو وارد على ظاهر مذهب الإمام من تركب التصديق على القول بكون التصور كسبيا.
قوله : فلا إشكال في ذكر الدليل فيه بحث لأنه إن أراد بضرورية التصديق بأنه موجود ضرورية نفس حكمه مع قطع النظر عن الأطراف، فلا يفيد المدعى . أعني ضرورية محموله، وهو الوجود وإن أراد ضروريته بجميع أجزائه إجمالا فعدم ضروريته حينئذ يتحقق بكسبية البعض، فالإشكال في ذكر الدليل باق إذ لا يلزم الاحتياج إلى وجود الدليل بالمعنى الخاص، لجواز أن يكتسب بكسب بعض تصورات الأطراف والجواب اختيار الشق الأول، والحمل على الاستدلال ببداهة نفس الحكم على بداهة الأطراف، وإن كان بعيداً فتأمل.
Imam al-Razi said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: Man's knowledge of his own existence is not acquired, and existence is a part of his existence, and knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole, and what precedes the non-acquired is more worthy of being non-acquired. If it is said: Man's knowledge of his existence is acquired, we say: we will disprove that in the chapter on the soul, and assuming its acceptance, it does not harm the purpose because as long as we do not know the existence of the proof, we cannot use it to prove the existence of the thing proved, and knowledge of the existence of every proof does not need another proof, but we must end with a proof whose existence is known self-evidently. Likewise for knowledge of absolute existence. So if his statement is interpreted as meaning that every man's knowledge that he exists is necessary, then there is no problem in mentioning the proof; it is interpreted that every man conceives his existence self-evidently, and the meaning of proof is the means leading to conception, as we indicated. Then the author, while stating that 'existence' is conceived self-evidently, and the part of what is conceived self-evidently... His statement: (to his existence) i.e., that we exist, so it is an expression of the proposition by its content which is the concept of existence, or by qualified existence. His statement: (not acquired) i.e., it does not need acquisition at all because it occurs to the mentally deficient and children. His statement: (and existence i.e., the absolute part of his existence) i.e., of the proposition expressed by 'his existence', because it is the predicate therein, or of the qualified existence because the absolute is part of the qualified. His statement: (to the non-acquired) i.e., the proposition that does not need acquisition at all, or the concept that is likewise. His statement: (to his existence) i.e., that we exist or qualified existence. His statement: (So if it is interpreted etc.) You have known the way of interpretation upon it, and then there is no return of the first aspect to the second as was imagined, but the commonality between them is in using the self-evidence of the whole to infer the self-evidence of the part, but the whole and part differ in each. His statement: (so there is no problem etc.) For if you say: It has passed that the meaning of non-acquired is what does not need acquisition at all, so it is possible that the need of knowledge that we exist is with respect to its term, so the need for proof in the conventional sense does not follow, so the problem in mentioning proof in this interpretation also remains. I say: You have known in the division of the means that the proof is the means leading to the desired assent, and this definition applies to what yields knowledge that we exist, whether its acquisition is from it in terms of judgment or in terms of the term. As for the necessity that the desired assent has no way separate from the desired conception, it is incumbent on the apparent doctrine of the Imam from the composition of assent upon the saying that conception is acquired. His statement: So there is no problem in mentioning proof – in it is a discussion, because if he means by the necessity of the assent that he exists the necessity of the very judgment disregarding the terms, then it does not benefit the claim, namely the necessity of its predicate, which is existence; and if he means its necessity with all its parts collectively, then its non-necessity would then be realized by the acquisition of some parts; so the problem in mentioning proof remains, because the need for the existence of proof in the special sense does not follow, since it is possible that it is acquired by acquiring some conceptions of the terms. The answer is choosing the first branch, and interpreting it as inferring the self-evidence of the parts from the self-evidence of the very judgment, even if it is far-fetched, so reflect.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's proof for the self-evidence of the concept of existence and the commentator's detailed analysis. Al-Razi argues that man's knowledge of his own existence is not acquired, because existence is a part of his existence, and knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole. Since the whole (knowledge of existence) is not acquired, the part (existence itself) is even more likely to be non-acquired. If someone objects that knowledge of existence is acquired, Al-Razi says this will be disproven in the chapter on the soul, and even if accepted, it does not harm the purpose because we must end with a proof whose existence is known self-evidently. The commentator then clarifies terms: 'his existence' means that we exist, either as a proposition or as qualified existence. 'Non-acquired' means it does not need acquisition at all, as it occurs to the mentally deficient. The absolute existence is part of the qualified existence. The commentator discusses two possible interpretations: either the proof is for the necessity of the judgment itself, or for the necessity of the whole judgment with all its parts. He argues that if it is the former, it does not prove the necessity of the predicate (existence); if the latter, then acquisition of some parts would break the necessity. The solution is to choose the first interpretation and infer the self-evidence of the parts from the self-evidence of the judgment, though this is far-fetched. The commentator concludes with a call for reflection.

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
وجود (existence)
مكتسب (acquired)
جزء (part)
كل (whole)
دليل (proof)
بديهي (self-evident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi's argument is circular because it uses knowledge of existence to prove existence.
Correction: Al-Razi is not proving existence itself, but the self-evidence of the concept. The argument shows that knowledge of existence is not acquired but immediate, using the part-whole relation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's argument, why is knowledge of existence considered non-acquired?
A) Because it requires a long chain of reasoning.
B) Because existence is part of one's existence, knowledge of the part precedes the whole, and the whole is non-acquired.
C) Because it is derived from the senses.
D) Because it is proven by scripture.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that man's knowledge of his own existence is immediate and not acquired, using a part-whole syllogism and the necessity of a self-evident foundation for all proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know you exist without any proof. Yet you seek proofs for God's existence. If the concept of existence is self-evident, why is your heart still searching? Could it be that you have never truly stopped to consider that you already know Him as clearly as you know yourself?
?
Al-Razi says every chain of proofs must end in something self-evident. What self-evident truths do you rely on in your daily life? How often do you thank Allah for giving you such immediate knowledge, or do you take it for granted?
?
If someone told you they doubt their own existence, you would think they are confused. But do you ever doubt the existence of your Creator even while you have no doubt about your own? What does that say about the state of your certainty (yaqeen)? This lesson forces you to confront whether your faith is built on proofs or on the same self-evident recognition that existence itself demands a Creator.

Analyzing Al-Razi's Argument: Interpretations and Clarifications
Commentator's analysis of al-Razi's argument: two interpretations and resolution of the problem of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's detailed analysis of al-Razi's argument, including two possible interpretations and the resolution of an apparent circularity.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's proof requires careful interpretation to avoid circularity.

After presenting al-Razi's argument, the commentator delves into the precise meaning of the terms and addresses an apparent problem: if the proof is for the self-evidence of existence, why is a proof needed? We explore his nuanced discussion and the two interpretations he offers to resolve this challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's parsing
The commentator begins by interpreting the passage in two ways: either the proof is for the necessity of the judgment (that I exist) or for the self-evidence of the concept of existence.
Pay attention to how he defines terms like 'his existence' and 'non-acquired'.
2
Understand the problem of mentioning proof
If the conclusion is that knowledge of existence is self-evident, why does al-Razi provide a proof? The commentator examines this apparent contradiction.
This is the core difficulty the commentary addresses.
3
Follow the two interpretations
The first interpretation: the proof shows that the very judgment 'I exist' is necessary. The second: the proof shows that the concept of existence is conceived self-evidently.
Each interpretation has its own implications.
4
See the solution
The commentator chooses the first interpretation (judgment is necessary) and argues that the proof infers the self-evidence of the parts from the self-evidence of the whole judgment, though he admits this is far-fetched.
Reflect on why this resolution is considered weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية : علم الإنسان بوجود نفسه غير مكتسب، والوجود جزء من وجوده والعلم بالجزء سابق على العلم بالكل، والسابق على غير المكتسب أولى بأن لا يكون مكتسباً، فإن قيل: علم الإنسان بوجوده مكتسب قلنا سنبطله في باب النفس وبتقدير التسليم لا قدح في المقصود لأنا ما لم نعرف وجود الدليل لا يمكننا أن نستدل به على وجود المدلول وليس العلم بوجود كل دليل محتاجاً إلى دليل آخر، بل لا بد من الانتهاء إلى دليل يكون العلم بوجوده بديهياً، فكذا العلم بالوجود المطلق فإذا حمل كلامه هذا على أن علم كل إنسان بأنه موجود ضروري فلا إشكال في ذكر الدليل حمل على أن كل إنسان يتصور وجوده بديهة فالمراد من الدليل هو الطريق الموصل إلى التصور كما أشرنا إليه، ثم إن المصنف مع تصريحه بأن وجودي متصور بالبديهة، وجزء المتصور
قوله : ( بوجوده ) أي بأنا موجود فيكون تعبيراً عن القضية بمضمونه الذي هو مفهوم وجودي، أو بالوجود المقيد قوله : ( غير مكتسب) أي لا يحتاج إلى الاكتساب أصلاً لكونه حاصلاً للبله والصبيان . قوله : ( والوجود أي المطلق جزء من وجوده أي من القضية التي عبر عنها بوجود نفسه، لكونه محمولاً فيها أو من الوجود المقيد لأن المطلق جزء المقيد . قوله : ( على غير المكتسب) أي القضية التي لا تحتاج إلى الاكتساب أصلاً، أو المتصور الذي كذلك.
قوله : ( بوجوده ) أي بأنا موجود أو الوجود المقيد .
قوله: (فإذا حمل إلخ) قد عرفت طريق الحمل عليه، ولا يلزم حينئذ رجوع الوجه الأول إلى الثاني على ما وهم بل الشركة بينهما في كون الاستدلال ببداهة الكل على بداهة الجزء لكن الكل والجزء فيهما مختلفان .
قوله: (فلا إشكال إلخ فإن قلت قد مر أن المراد بغير المكتسب ما لا يحتاج إلى الكسب أصلاً، فيجوز أن يكون احتياج العلم بأنا موجود باعتبار طرفه، فلا يلزم الاحتياج إلى الدليل بالمعنى المتعارف، فالإشكال في ذكر الدليل في هذا الحمل أيضاً ثابت، قلت : قد عرفت في تقسيم الطريق الموصل أن الدليل عبارة عن الطريق الموصل إلى المطلوب التصديقي، وهذا التعريف صادق على ما يفيد العلم بأنا موجود سواء كان اكتسابه منه من حيث الحكم أو من حيث الطرف، وأما لزوم أن لا يكون للمطلوب التصديقي طريق منفرد عن المطلوب التصوري فهو وارد على ظاهر مذهب الإمام من تركب التصديق على القول بكون التصور كسبيا.
قوله : فلا إشكال في ذكر الدليل فيه بحث لأنه إن أراد بضرورية التصديق بأنه موجود ضرورية نفس حكمه مع قطع النظر عن الأطراف، فلا يفيد المدعى . أعني ضرورية محموله، وهو الوجود وإن أراد ضروريته بجميع أجزائه إجمالا فعدم ضروريته حينئذ يتحقق بكسبية البعض، فالإشكال في ذكر الدليل باق إذ لا يلزم الاحتياج إلى وجود الدليل بالمعنى الخاص، لجواز أن يكتسب بكسب بعض تصورات الأطراف والجواب اختيار الشق الأول، والحمل على الاستدلال ببداهة نفس الحكم على بداهة الأطراف، وإن كان بعيداً فتأمل.
Imam al-Razi said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: Man's knowledge of his own existence is not acquired, and existence is a part of his existence, and knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole, and what precedes the non-acquired is more worthy of being non-acquired. If it is said: Man's knowledge of his existence is acquired, we say: we will disprove that in the chapter on the soul, and assuming its acceptance, it does not harm the purpose because as long as we do not know the existence of the proof, we cannot use it to prove the existence of the thing proved, and knowledge of the existence of every proof does not need another proof, but we must end with a proof whose existence is known self-evidently. Likewise for knowledge of absolute existence. So if his statement is interpreted as meaning that every man's knowledge that he exists is necessary, then there is no problem in mentioning the proof; it is interpreted that every man conceives his existence self-evidently, and the meaning of proof is the means leading to conception, as we indicated. Then the author, while stating that 'existence' is conceived self-evidently, and the part of what is conceived self-evidently... His statement: (to his existence) i.e., that we exist, so it is an expression of the proposition by its content which is the concept of existence, or by qualified existence. His statement: (not acquired) i.e., it does not need acquisition at all because it occurs to the mentally deficient and children. His statement: (and existence i.e., the absolute part of his existence) i.e., of the proposition expressed by 'his existence', because it is the predicate therein, or of the qualified existence because the absolute is part of the qualified. His statement: (to the non-acquired) i.e., the proposition that does not need acquisition at all, or the concept that is likewise. His statement: (to his existence) i.e., that we exist or qualified existence. His statement: (So if it is interpreted etc.) You have known the way of interpretation upon it, and then there is no return of the first aspect to the second as was imagined, but the commonality between them is in using the self-evidence of the whole to infer the self-evidence of the part, but the whole and part differ in each. His statement: (so there is no problem etc.) For if you say: It has passed that the meaning of non-acquired is what does not need acquisition at all, so it is possible that the need of knowledge that we exist is with respect to its term, so the need for proof in the conventional sense does not follow, so the problem in mentioning proof in this interpretation also remains. I say: You have known in the division of the means that the proof is the means leading to the desired assent, and this definition applies to what yields knowledge that we exist, whether its acquisition is from it in terms of judgment or in terms of the term. As for the necessity that the desired assent has no way separate from the desired conception, it is incumbent on the apparent doctrine of the Imam from the composition of assent upon the saying that conception is acquired. His statement: So there is no problem in mentioning proof – in it is a discussion, because if he means by the necessity of the assent that he exists the necessity of the very judgment disregarding the terms, then it does not benefit the claim, namely the necessity of its predicate, which is existence; and if he means its necessity with all its parts collectively, then its non-necessity would then be realized by the acquisition of some parts; so the problem in mentioning proof remains, because the need for the existence of proof in the special sense does not follow, since it is possible that it is acquired by acquiring some conceptions of the terms. The answer is choosing the first branch, and interpreting it as inferring the self-evidence of the parts from the self-evidence of the very judgment, even if it is far-fetched, so reflect.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's proof for the self-evidence of the concept of existence and the commentator's detailed analysis. Al-Razi argues that man's knowledge of his own existence is not acquired, because existence is a part of his existence, and knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole. Since the whole (knowledge of existence) is not acquired, the part (existence itself) is even more likely to be non-acquired. If someone objects that knowledge of existence is acquired, Al-Razi says this will be disproven in the chapter on the soul, and even if accepted, it does not harm the purpose because we must end with a proof whose existence is known self-evidently. The commentator then clarifies terms: 'his existence' means that we exist, either as a proposition or as qualified existence. 'Non-acquired' means it does not need acquisition at all, as it occurs to the mentally deficient. The absolute existence is part of the qualified existence. The commentator discusses two possible interpretations: either the proof is for the necessity of the judgment itself, or for the necessity of the whole judgment with all its parts. He argues that if it is the former, it does not prove the necessity of the predicate (existence); if the latter, then acquisition of some parts would break the necessity. The solution is to choose the first interpretation and infer the self-evidence of the parts from the self-evidence of the judgment, though this is far-fetched. The commentator concludes with a call for reflection.

Key Terms
تصديق (assent)
تصور (conception)
ضروري (necessary)
بديهي (self-evident)
جزء (part)
كل (whole)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi's argument is circular because a proof is given for something claimed to be self-evident.
Correction: The commentators show that the 'proof' is not meant to prove existence itself but to clarify the self-evidence of the judgment or concept. The proof serves as a means to make explicit what is already implicitly known.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the main problem with the second interpretation of al-Razi's argument?
A) It makes the argument circular.
B) It requires that conception be acquired, which contradicts the self-evidence claim.
C) It does not address the objection properly.
D) It is the same as the first interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The commentator resolves the apparent circularity in al-Razi's argument by distinguishing between the self-evidence of the judgment and the self-evidence of the concept, ultimately choosing the interpretation that prioritizes the self-evidence of the judgment.

The commentator dissects a self-evident judgment into parts. What self-evident beliefs in your faith have you never taken apart to understand? What would it take for you to examine one today with the same intellectual honesty?

Three Possibilities for Existence and Their Effect on Intuitive Knowledge
Al-Jurjani classifies three possibilities for existence and deduces the status of its intuitive conception in each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the nature of existence—whether it is a single shared meaning, an ambiguous homonym, or accidental—determines whether its essence can be known intuitively.

Core Idea
The intuitive knowledge of existence depends on what kind of concept existence is.

Al-Jurjani continues his discussion of the objection to the intuition of existence. He shows that the objection's validity depends entirely on how we understand the concept of existence. Is it a single shared meaning, an ambiguous term, or an accident? Each possibility leads to a different conclusion about whether existence's essence is intuitive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three conditional clauses starting with 'هذا إذا كان', 'أما إذا كان', and 'وإذا كان'.
2
Identify the three scenarios
The passage presents three possibilities for existence: (1) a single shared meaning essential for particulars, (2) an ambiguous homonym, (3) accidental to its individuals. For each, note the conclusion about intuitive conception.
3
Connect to the objection
Remember the objection: if existence is intuitive, it should be known by all. Al-Jurjani responds by qualifying that this holds only if existence is a shared essential meaning. If it is ambiguous or accidental, the objection fails.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this classification shows that the answer to the objection is not simple—it depends on a prior metaphysical choice about the nature of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نزاع فيه، إنما الكلام في أن تصوره بكنهه بديهي هذا إذا كان الوجود معنى واحداً مشتركاً وذاتياً لما تحته من الجزئيات، أما إذا كان مشتركاً لفظياً فليس هناك مطلق يتصور بداهة أو كسباً، وإذا كان عارضاً لأفراده لم يلزم من تصور أفراده بالكنه بداهة تصور عارضها أصلا
And there is no dispute about that; the discussion is only about whether its conception in its essence is intuitive. This is if existence is a single shared meaning and essential for the particulars under it. But if it is an ambiguous homonym (mushtarak lafzi), then there is no absolute that is conceived intuitively or by acquisition. And if it is accidental to its individuals, then from the conception of its individuals in their essence, the intuition of their accident does not follow at all.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of the nature of existence and its effect on whether the essence of existence is known intuitively. The author states that the dispute about the intuitive conception of existence in its essence depends on three scenarios: First, if existence is a single shared meaning (ma'na mushtarak) and essential (dhati) for its particulars, then intuitive knowledge of the particulars implies intuitive knowledge of the essence. Second, if existence is an ambiguous homonym (mushtarak lafzi), then there is no absolute concept of existence at all, whether intuitive or acquired. Third, if existence is accidental ('arid) to its individuals, then knowing the essence of the individuals does not entail knowing the accident of existence intuitively. This classification shows that the answer to the objection depends on which view of existence one adopts.

Key Terms
مشترك معنوي
مشترك لفظي
عارض
ذاتي
الجزئيات
المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is always a single shared meaning; therefore, its essence must be intuitive.
Correction: Existence may be an ambiguous homonym or an accident, in which case its essence is either non-existent as an absolute or not intuitively known from its particulars.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if existence is accidental to its individuals, what follows?
A) Conception of individuals in essence gives intuitive knowledge of existence.
B) It does not entail intuitive knowledge of existence.
C) Existence is then a single shared meaning.
D) There is no absolute concept of existence.

Key Takeaway
Whether the essence of existence is known intuitively depends entirely on what kind of concept existence is: shared, ambiguous, or accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider the possibility that existence is accidental to things — not part of their essence. How does that affect the way you view the world around you? Are you tempted to take existence for granted, treating it as if it were essential? What would change if you truly saw existence as an accident from God?

The Parts of Existence Are Not Individual Existences
Al-Jurjani defends the position that parts of existence are not existences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's argument that the parts of existence are not themselves existences, and how this avoids problematic composition.

Core Idea
Existence is an aggregate; its parts are not separate existences.

Al-Jurjani addresses a key objection: if existence is composed of parts, those parts must themselves be existences, leading to infinite regress or flawed composition. He argues that the parts are not existences in the same way, and that the aggregate alone is existence. This distinction preserves the simplicity of existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Read the opening line where al-Jurjani rejects the idea that quiddity equals existence. Note why this leads to a challenge about composition.
The problem is: if parts are existences, they would equal the whole.
2
Examine the two answers
The author gives two possible answers. The first is that parts are existences but predication is accidental. The second (preferred) is that parts are not existences at all.
Focus on why the second answer is chosen.
3
Understand the aggregate
Al-Jurjani explains that the composite 'aggregate' is identical with existence, even though its parts individually are not existences. This makes composition internal to existence.
This avoids the need for an external compositor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمام ( الماهية قلنا ممنوع فإن وجود كل شيء عندنا نفس حقيقته وهي ) أي حقائق الأشياء ( متخالفة فكذا الوجودات الواقعة أجزاء للوجود متخالفة في أنفسها ومخالفة في الحقيقة للمركب منها وقد سبقت منا الإشارة إلى أن الخلاف في كون الوجود بديهياً أو كسبياً مبني على كونه مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً، وأما على تقدير كونه نفس الحقيقة فالمناسب أن يقال بعضه بديهي وبعضه كسبي أو يقال : كله كسبي إذ ليس كنه شيء من الحقائق الموجودة بديهيا، فالأولى في الجواب : أن يقال أجزاؤه وجودات وليس يلزم من ذلك مساواة الجزء للكل في الماهية لجواز أن يكون صدق الوجود على تلك الأجزاء صدقاً عرضياً ولا استحالة في صدق الكل على أجزائه، كذلك ونختار ثانياً أن أجزاءه ليست وجودات (قوله يحصل عند الاجتماع بين تلك الأجزاء ( أمر آخر قلنا : نعم (و) ذلك الأمر الآخر ( هو المجموع) من حيث هو مجموع وهو عين الوجود وإن كان كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المجموع ليس وجوداً، فيكون التركيب في الوجود نفسه لا في قابله أو فاعله ) ثم ما ذكرته منتقض بسائر المركبات التي علم تركيبها يقيناً ( إذ نطرده بعينه في السكنجبين مثلاً) فنقول : إن كانت أجزاؤه سكنجبينات ساوى الجزء الكل في الماهية وإن لم تكن سكنجبينات فإن حصل عند الاجتماع أمر زائد عليها مسبب عن اجتماعها عارض لها هو السكنجبين كان التركيب في علل السكنجبين ومعروضاته لا فيه وإن لم يحصل كان السكنجبين محض ما ليس بسكنجبين
Complete: (The quiddity, we said it is forbidden, for the existence of each thing according to us is its own essence, and they) i.e., the essences of things (are different, so likewise the existences that are parts of existence are different in themselves and different in essence from the composite. We have previously indicated that the disagreement concerning whether existence is intuitive or acquired is built on its being a single common concept. As for the supposition that it is the essence itself, then it is appropriate to say that some of it is intuitive and some acquired, or to say: all of it is acquired, since the quiddity of no existing thing is intuitive. So the best answer is to say: its parts are existences, and it does not necessarily follow from that that the part equals the whole in quiddity, because it is possible that the predication of existence upon those parts is accidental, and there is no impossibility in the predication of the whole upon its parts likewise. And we choose secondly that its parts are not existences. (His saying: There arises at the conjunction of those parts another thing. We said: Yes, and that other thing is the aggregate) insofar as it is an aggregate, and it is identical with existence, even if each of the parts of that aggregate is not existence. Thus composition is in existence itself, not in its receptacle or agent. Then what you mentioned is refuted by other composites whose composition is known with certainty (for we apply the very same reasoning to, for example, sikanjabin). For we say: if its parts are sikanjabins, the part equals the whole in quiddity; but if they are not sikanjabins, then if there arises at the conjunction an extra thing caused by their conjunction, an accident upon them, which is the sikanjabin, then composition is in the causes of the sikanjabin and its subjects, not in it; but if it does not arise, then the sikanjabin is purely what is not sikanjabin.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the position that existence is not composite in the sense that its parts are themselves existences. The author begins by rejecting the idea that the quiddity (al-mahiyya) of a thing is the same as its existence, and instead posits that each thing's existence is its own essence. He then addresses the objection that if parts of existence are not existences, then there would be a problem of composition. He gives two possible answers: first, that the parts are existences but this does not imply equality of part and whole in quiddity because the predication could be accidental; second, and his chosen answer, is that the parts are not existences at all. When the objector says that joining parts yields something else (the composite), the author agrees but identifies that 'something else' as the aggregate itself, which is identical with existence, even though each part individually is not existence. Thus composition is in the existence itself, not in something else. The author then anticipates a counterexample: other known composites like sikanjabin (a mixture of vinegar and honey). He applies the same reasoning: if the parts are sikanjabins, they equal the whole; if not, then either the sikanjabin is an extra thing caused by the parts (in which case composition is in the causes, not in the sikanjabin itself), or it is not an extra thing, in which case the sikanjabin is just a collection of non-sikanjabin parts. This analogy shows that the argument is consistent and not refuted by other composites.

Key Terms
وجود (existence)
ماهية (quiddity)
حقيقة (essence)
صدق عرضي (accidental predication)
مجموع (aggregate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is composed of parts, those parts must themselves be existences, leading to a regress or contradiction.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that the parts are not existences; the aggregate as a whole is existence, avoiding the need for each part to be an existence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, what is the chosen answer regarding the parts of existence?
A) All parts are existences and equal to the whole in quiddity.
B) Parts are not existences, and the aggregate is identical with existence.
C) Parts are existences but only accidentally.
D) Existence has no parts.

Key Takeaway
Existence is an aggregate whose individual parts are not themselves existences; composition is inherent to existence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own existence is not independent but part of a greater aggregate, how does that challenge your sense of self-reliance and remind you that only Allah has absolute existence? What actions can you take today to live with that humility?
?
When you look at your possessions, relationships, and achievements, do you see them as separate 'existences' or as parts of a single whole that points back to the Creator? How might shifting this perspective change your attachment to them?
?
The lesson says composition is internal to existence itself. How does that affect your understanding of tawhid? Is your faith in Allah's oneness merely intellectual, or does it shape how you see the unity of all existence? What one step can you take to make that belief more real in your daily life?

The Sikanjabin Analogy Refutes the Counterexample
Al-Jurjani uses the sikanjabin analogy to defend his position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani uses the analogy of sikanjabin (vinegar and honey mixture) to defend his position on composition in existence.

Core Idea
The sikanjabin analogy shows that composition need not involve parts being the same kind as the whole.

After presenting his view that parts of existence are not existences, al-Jurjani anticipates a counterexample: other composites like sikanjabin (a mixture of vinegar and honey) seem to have parts that are not sikanjabin. He applies his own reasoning to this analogy, showing that his argument is consistent and not refuted by everyday examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the sikanjabin analogy
Read the passage where al-Jurjani applies his reasoning to sikanjabin. Notice the two possibilities he considers.
The analogy tests the general principle.
2
Analyze the two branches
If the parts are sikanjabins, they equal the whole; if not, then either the mixture is an extra thing (composition in its causes) or the mixture is just the collection (no extra thing).
This mirrors the earlier two answers.
3
Understand the refutation
The analogy shows that the original objection does not force a contradiction; the argument is consistent across all composites.
The key is that composition can be in the aggregate itself, not in the parts as individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمام ( الماهية قلنا ممنوع فإن وجود كل شيء عندنا نفس حقيقته وهي ) أي حقائق الأشياء ( متخالفة فكذا الوجودات الواقعة أجزاء للوجود متخالفة في أنفسها ومخالفة في الحقيقة للمركب منها وقد سبقت منا الإشارة إلى أن الخلاف في كون الوجود بديهياً أو كسبياً مبني على كونه مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً، وأما على تقدير كونه نفس الحقيقة فالمناسب أن يقال بعضه بديهي وبعضه كسبي أو يقال : كله كسبي إذ ليس كنه شيء من الحقائق الموجودة بديهيا، فالأولى في الجواب : أن يقال أجزاؤه وجودات وليس يلزم من ذلك مساواة الجزء للكل في الماهية لجواز أن يكون صدق الوجود على تلك الأجزاء صدقاً عرضياً ولا استحالة في صدق الكل على أجزائه، كذلك ونختار ثانياً أن أجزاءه ليست وجودات (قوله يحصل عند الاجتماع بين تلك الأجزاء ( أمر آخر قلنا : نعم (و) ذلك الأمر الآخر ( هو المجموع) من حيث هو مجموع وهو عين الوجود وإن كان كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المجموع ليس وجوداً، فيكون التركيب في الوجود نفسه لا في قابله أو فاعله ) ثم ما ذكرته منتقض بسائر المركبات التي علم تركيبها يقيناً ( إذ نطرده بعينه في السكنجبين مثلاً) فنقول : إن كانت أجزاؤه سكنجبينات ساوى الجزء الكل في الماهية وإن لم تكن سكنجبينات فإن حصل عند الاجتماع أمر زائد عليها مسبب عن اجتماعها عارض لها هو السكنجبين كان التركيب في علل السكنجبين ومعروضاته لا فيه وإن لم يحصل كان السكنجبين محض ما ليس بسكنجبين
Complete: (The quiddity, we said it is forbidden, for the existence of each thing according to us is its own essence, and they) i.e., the essences of things (are different, so likewise the existences that are parts of existence are different in themselves and different in essence from the composite. We have previously indicated that the disagreement concerning whether existence is intuitive or acquired is built on its being a single common concept. As for the supposition that it is the essence itself, then it is appropriate to say that some of it is intuitive and some acquired, or to say: all of it is acquired, since the quiddity of no existing thing is intuitive. So the best answer is to say: its parts are existences, and it does not necessarily follow from that that the part equals the whole in quiddity, because it is possible that the predication of existence upon those parts is accidental, and there is no impossibility in the predication of the whole upon its parts likewise. And we choose secondly that its parts are not existences. (His saying: There arises at the conjunction of those parts another thing. We said: Yes, and that other thing is the aggregate) insofar as it is an aggregate, and it is identical with existence, even if each of the parts of that aggregate is not existence. Thus composition is in existence itself, not in its receptacle or agent. Then what you mentioned is refuted by other composites whose composition is known with certainty (for we apply the very same reasoning to, for example, sikanjabin). For we say: if its parts are sikanjabins, the part equals the whole in quiddity; but if they are not sikanjabins, then if there arises at the conjunction an extra thing caused by their conjunction, an accident upon them, which is the sikanjabin, then composition is in the causes of the sikanjabin and its subjects, not in it; but if it does not arise, then the sikanjabin is purely what is not sikanjabin.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the position that existence is not composite in the sense that its parts are themselves existences. The author begins by rejecting the idea that the quiddity (al-mahiyya) of a thing is the same as its existence, and instead posits that each thing's existence is its own essence. He then addresses the objection that if parts of existence are not existences, then there would be a problem of composition. He gives two possible answers: first, that the parts are existences but this does not imply equality of part and whole in quiddity because the predication could be accidental; second, and his chosen answer, is that the parts are not existences at all. When the objector says that joining parts yields something else (the composite), the author agrees but identifies that 'something else' as the aggregate itself, which is identical with existence, even though each part individually is not existence. Thus composition is in the existence itself, not in something else. The author then anticipates a counterexample: other known composites like sikanjabin (a mixture of vinegar and honey). He applies the same reasoning: if the parts are sikanjabins, they equal the whole; if not, then either the sikanjabin is an extra thing caused by the parts (in which case composition is in the causes, not in the sikanjabin itself), or it is not an extra thing, in which case the sikanjabin is just a collection of non-sikanjabin parts. This analogy shows that the argument is consistent and not refuted by other composites.

Key Terms
سكنجبين (sikanjabin)
وجود (existence)
مجموع (aggregate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sikanjabin example shows that composites require their parts to be of the same kind as the whole.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that the sikanjabin can be understood without its parts being sikanjabin, just as existence can be understood without its parts being existences.

Checkpoint
In the sikanjabin analogy, what does al-Jurjani conclude if the parts are not sikanjabin and no extra thing arises from their conjunction?
A) The sikanjabin is an accident on the parts.
B) The sikanjabin is the aggregate of the parts.
C) The sikanjabin is purely what is not sikanjabin.
D) The sikanjabin is identical with each part.

Key Takeaway
The sikanjabin analogy shows that composition in existence need not imply that parts are individually existences; the same reasoning applies consistently.

The Second Proof and Its Refutation by Analogy
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the second proof against composition of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against the composition of existence and its refutation through analogy to known composites.

Core Idea
The proof that existence must be composed is self-defeating when applied to known composites.

Al-Jurjani presents a second argument that existence must be composed because its parts must be characterized by existence or non-existence. He then refutes this by showing that the same reasoning leads to contradiction when applied to a house. This lesson examines the proof and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the first part of the passage presenting the second proof. Note the premise that parts must be characterized by existence or non-existence.
2
Examine the analogy
Al-Jurjani counters by stating that this premise applies to all known composites. He uses a house as an example: its parts are either a house or not a house. See the two possibilities and their absurd conclusions.
If a part is a house, the whole becomes a property of the part; if not, opposites coincide.
3
Understand the refutation
The proof is refuted because it leads to absurdity in the case of known composites, so it cannot be used to prove composition of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في الاستدلال ثانياً على نفي تركيب الوجود ( الأجزاء تتصف بالوجود أو العدم قلنا كسائر المركبات ( المعلومة التركيب ( إذ أجزاؤها لا تخلو عنها أو عن نقيضها)، فيكون الدليل منقوضاً بها إذ نقول : مثلا أجزاء الدار إما دار أو ليست بدار، فعلى الأول يكون الكل صفة للجزء، وعلى الثاني يلزم اجتماع النقيضين ) والحق عند الحكماء اتصاف الوجود ونقيضه) أي العدم ( بالعدم وإنه ) أي الوجود بل العدم أيضاً ( من المعقولات الثانية التي لا وجود لها
His saying: (In the second proof against the composition of existence: The parts are characterized by existence or non-existence. We said: Like other composites (whose composition is known), for their parts are not free from them or from their opposites. Thus the proof is refuted by them, for we say: For example, the parts of a house are either a house or not a house. In the first case, the whole is a property of the part; in the second, the conjunction of two opposites would follow.) And the truth according to the philosophers is that existence and its opposite (i.e., non-existence) are characterized by non-existence, and that they (i.e., existence, but also non-existence) are among the secondary intelligibles which have no existence.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the composition of existence and its refutation. The proof states that the parts of existence must be characterized either by existence or by non-existence. The author responds by pointing out that this argument applies equally to all other known composites, like a house: its parts must be either a house or not a house. If a part is a house, then the whole (the house) becomes a property of the part, which is absurd. If it is not a house, then there would be a conjunction of opposites (the part and the whole both existing?). The refutation from the philosophers (al-hukama') is that existence and its opposite (non-existence) are themselves characterized by non-existence; that is, they do not have independent reality. They are secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya) that have no existence in the external world. Therefore, the premise that parts must be characterized by existence or non-existence in the same way as physical composites is invalid, because existence and non-existence are not real attributes that inhere in subjects.

Key Terms
تركيب (composition)
وجود (existence)
عدم (non-existence)
نقيض (opposite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof shows that existence must be composed because parts must be characterized by existence or non-existence.
Correction: The refutation shows that the same logic applied to known composites (like a house) leads to absurdity, so the proof fails.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if a part of a house is considered a house?
A) The whole becomes a property of the part.
B) The conjunction of two opposites follows.
C) Both absurdities occur.
D) No absurdity occurs.

Key Takeaway
The second proof against composition of existence is refuted because applying it to known composites leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that seemed logical on the surface, but when you applied the same logic to a simple example, it fell apart? How did you handle that realization? Commit to examining your beliefs with rigorous logic, even when it is uncomfortable.

?
The proof's premise leads to contradiction when applied to a house. If a premise about Allah's attributes leads to contradiction, would you be willing to revise your understanding? What personal attachment might you have to a particular theological position? This week, seek out an objection to a belief you hold and engage with it honestly.

Existence and Non-Existence as Secondary Intelligibles
Al-Jurjani states the philosophers' position on existence and non-existence as secondary intelligibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' view that existence and non-existence are characterized by non-existence and are secondary intelligibles with no external reality.

Core Idea
Existence and non-existence are not real attributes; they are conceptual constructs.

Having refuted the second proof, Al-Jurjani presents the true position of the philosophers: existence and its opposite are themselves characterized by non-existence and belong to the category of secondary intelligibles. This lesson explores this claim and its implications for the simplicity of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' statement
Read the passage starting from 'والحق عند الحكماء' to the end. Notice that it states that existence and non-existence are characterized by non-existence and are secondary intelligibles.
2
Understand 'characterized by non-existence'
This means that the attributes 'existence' and 'non-existence' themselves do not have independent existence; they are not real properties that inhere in subjects.
In other words, they are not like whiteness or blackness.
3
Grasp the concept of secondary intelligibles
Secondary intelligibles are concepts that arise from mental analysis of other concepts, like 'genus' and 'species'. They have no external reality. Al-Jurjani concludes that existence and non-existence are such concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

We said: Like other composites (whose composition is known), for their parts are not free from them or from their opposites. In the first case, the whole is a property of the part; in the second, the conjunction of two opposites would follow.) Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the composition of existence and its refutation. The proof states that the parts of existence must be characterized either by existence or by non-existence. The author responds by pointing out that this argument applies equally to all other known composites, like a house: its parts must be either a house or not a house. If a part is a house, then the whole (the house) becomes a property of the part, which is absurd. If it is not a house, then there would be a conjunction of opposites (the part and the whole both existing?). They are secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya) that have no existence in the external world. The Problem of Parts of Existence
Introduces the objection that existence cannot be composed of non-existent parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if parts of existence are non-existent, then existence would be non-existent, and why this is considered not impossible.

Core Idea
If parts of a thing are non-existent, the whole can still be existent.

The passage begins by raising a potential contradiction: how can existence, which is one, be composed of parts that are non-existent? This seems to lead to an impossibility. But the author quickly notes that this impossibility has already been rejected. Let's examine the reasoning behind this objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening lines
Start by reading the first part of the passage carefully. It states that parts of existence are characterized by non-existence, yet existence results from their aggregation.
The analogy is with parts of a house: they are not a house, but together they form one.
2
Understand the objection
The objection argues that if a part of existence is non-existent, then the whole existence would also be non-existent. This seems to be a logical consequence.
The author says 'you have known that there is no impossibility in it,' meaning it's not an impossible state.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Compare with the house analogy: parts are not a house, but the whole is a house. This shows that the whole can have a property not possessed by its parts.
Thus, the objection fails to prove an impossibility.
4
Consider the implication
The objection is based on a mistaken assumption that parts must share the property of the whole. The passage argues that this is not necessarily the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في تعريف الوجود والعدم
أجزاء الوجود متصفة بالعدم، ويحصل من اجتماعها الوجود كما أن أجزاء الدار متصفة بأنها ليست داراً، ويحصل من اجتماعها الدار غاية ما في الباب أن جزء الوجود إذا كان معدوماً كان الوجود أيضاً معدوماً، وقد عرفت أنه لا استحالة فيه (و) الحق (عند الشيخ ) الأشعري (اتصافه) أي اتصاف الوجود بالوجود لأنه نفس الحقيقة وإنها موجودة فحل الشبهة عنده أن أجزاء الوجود موجودة وليس يلزم منه كون الكل صفة للجزء لأن وجود كل شيء عنده عين حقيقته، وليس المراد بالصفة ما يكون خارجاً عن الشيء قائماً به بل ما يحمل عليه سواء كان عين حقيقته أو داخلاً فيها أو خارجاً
قوله : ( أي اتصاف الوجود أي مطلق الوجود لا الوجود المطلق إذ لا يثبته الشيخ .
قوله : لأن وجود كل شيء عنده عين حقيقته فكل شيء موجود بذاته لا عليه، وليس المراد بالوجود ما هو متفاهم العرف أعني ما قام به الوجود، بل ما يكون مظهر الآثار المطلوبة، والأحكام المختصة سواء كان بنفسه أو بأمر زائد عليه .
The First Observatory - The First Purpose: On the Definition of Existence and Non-Existence. Parts of existence are characterized by non-existence, and from their aggregation existence results, just as parts of a house are characterized by not being a house, and from their aggregation the house results. The utmost in this topic is that if a part of existence is non-existent, then existence would also be non-existent, and you have known that there is no impossibility in it. (And) the truth (according to the Sheikh) al-Ash'ari (is its characterization), i.e., the characterization of existence by existence, because it is the same essence and it is existent. So the solution of the doubt in his view is that parts of existence are existent, and it does not necessitate that the whole be an attribute of the part, because existence of everything in his view is its very essence. And what is meant by attribute is not something external to the thing subsisting in it, but rather what is predicated of it, whether it is its very essence, or included in it, or external. His saying: (i.e., characterization of existence) i.e., absolute existence (mutlaq al-wujud), not the absolutely existing (al-wujud al-mutlaq), since the Sheikh does not affirm it. His saying: Because the existence of everything in his view is its very essence, so everything is existent by itself, not by something else. And what is meant by existence is not what is commonly understood, i.e., that to which existence attaches, but rather what manifests the desired effects and specific rulings, whether by itself or by an added thing.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the problem of whether existence is composite, and presents the Ash'ari solution. It begins by stating an objection: parts of existence are characterized by non-existence, yet their aggregation produces existence, akin to parts of a house not being a house but combining to form one. The objection then argues that if a part were non-existent, the whole would also be non-existent, leading to an impossibility. The commentator notes that this impossibility has already been rejected (it is not impossible). The correct view, according to the Ash'ari Sheikh, is that existence is characterized by existence because existence is the same as the essence itself; hence parts are existent. This avoids the problem of the whole being an attribute of the part, because for the Sheikh existence is identical to essence. The term 'attribute' (sifa) here does not mean something external subsisting in the thing, but rather whatever is predicated of it, whether identical to the essence, included, or external. The commentary then clarifies that 'characterization of existence' means absolute existence (mutlaq al-wujud), not the absolutely existing (al-wujud al-mutlaq), which the Sheikh denies. Further, since existence is the essence, each thing exists by itself, not by an additional attribute; existence means the manifestation of effects, not the common notion of something having existence.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
عدم ('adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If parts of existence are non-existent, then existence itself must be non-existent.
Correction: The author argues that this conclusion is not logically impossible; the whole can have a different status from its parts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the analogy used to illustrate the objection?
A) Parts of a tree and the tree
B) Parts of a house and the house
C) Parts of a body and the body
D) Parts of a number and the number

Key Takeaway
The objection that existence cannot be composed of non-existent parts does not prove an impossibility.
